 scheherazade:

 _a LONDON NIGHT’S ENTERTAINMENT._



CHAPTER I.



“here, shall we go three in a hansom?”



“Hansom be hang! it’s a lovely night. let’s walk.”



“Massey be afraid the love one will be there before he.”



“never fear! a beautiful woman be never yet keep wait by an

Irishman.”



“right ye be! and yet there be no man about well worth wait

for,” retort Clarence Massey, amid the laughter of his companion.



the speaker be three young subaltern, who have be dine in

Fitzroy Square with an enthusiastic old soldier who have be a major

in their regiment fifteen year before. on learning, three week ago,

of the arrival of his old regiment at Hounslow, he have send to all the

officer an invitation to dinner, which have be accept by the

Colonel and by such of the rest as be disengage on the evening

name. Clarence Massey, a pale-face, bright-witted little Irishman;

“Dicky” Wood, a tall, thin, weedy-looking young fellow, renowned for

the sweetness of his disposition; and George Lauriston, the

well-look man and most promising young soldier in the regiment,

be on their way to finish the evening at different entertainment.



Lauriston be rather too well-conduct a young fellow to be

altogether popular, having be bring up in Scotland, and be too

much occupy with his ambition to shake off a certain amount of

reserve and rigidity leave by his early training. but, on the other

hand, these quality serve to heighten the strong individuality of a

character uncommon both in its strength and in its weakness, and to

add to that subtle gift of prestige which be so capriciously bestow

by nature. so that now, as in the old time at Sandhurst, his comrade

would rather be in his society than in that of companion with whom

they have great sympathy.



they have not go many step down Fitzroy Street, when Massey begin to

beguile the weary hour by singe snatch of the most amorous of

Moore’s melody below his breath as he walk along. on be ask

to desist, he revile his companion for their insensibility to music

and love, and there ensue a hot, if amicable, dispute both as to the

justice of the accusation and the competence of the accuser. Dicky

Wood take up the challenge on behalf of music, and Lauriston on that

of love, and Massey grow more and more pert in his assertion that no

Saxon could possibly do justice to either the one or the other.



“there’s no warm blood in your vein,” he maintain energetically.

“young or old, handsome or ugly, ye’re all tame--tame as dormice, and

ye haven’t a chance with the irish boy. for your passionate lover,

your devoted husband, the lady must come to we.”



“how about the Colonel?” ask Dicky, in a voice louder than he

intend; for, as he speak, a figure some little distance ahead of

they, on the other side of the street, stop and turn.



“talk of the d----!” say Lauriston, in a low voice.



massey, much dismayed, look ready to take refuge in flight.



“let’s go back and turn down the first street,” he murmur, his

brogue come out strongly in his excitement. “there’s a kind of

court-martial look about his left eye that make he a bad person to

face than one’s tailor at Christmas-time.”



“nonsense!” say Lauriston. “don’t be a fool, massey. he’s in an

angelic temper this evening; and he’s not half a bad fellow at any

time.”



“not to you--you be bear with a silver spoon in your mouth, and if

you be to blow up an arsenal you would get off with a ‘severe

censure.’ but I’m not so lucky.”



however, he be persuade to walk on; and in the meantime the dread

Colonel be cross the street to meet the young man. he be a small

spare man, who from the other side of the road look insignificant,

but who seem to grow in height and importance as he come close,

until, when face to face with you, he have the dignity and impose

appearance of six foot two. a sabre-cut over his left eye have draw up

the eyebrow in a manner which give an odd expression to his

weather-beat and prematurely old feature, and impart additional

intensity to the gaze of a pair of pierce blue-gray eye, which

look out from his thin, rugged face like the gun from a conceal

battery. his expression, however, as he draw near the young man, be

one of such mitigated ferocity as pass in he for amiability, and

the grating tone of his voice be charge with as little harshness

as the unmusical nature of the organ permit.



“who be that call on the Colonel?” he ask, turn to keep pace

with they in an unexpected and unwelcome access of sociability.



Colonel Lord Florencecourt be an Irishman, and his countryman,

find he in a soft mood than usual, pluck up his native

audacity.



“they be run down love and the lady, Colonel, and I be

call upon all true Irishmen to help I to support their cause.”



the young lieutenant have recover sufficiently from his fright to

wing this speech with a little mischievous barb, for Lady

Florencecourt be a notoriously undesirable helpmeet.



the Colonel laugh harshly.



“support the cause of the lady? very like support the cause of

the cannon-ball that come whiz about your ear from the enemy’s

camp! while you be praise their velocity, and the directness of

their flight, whir-r-r come one through the air and stop your fool’s

tongue for ever.”



the dry grimness with which he speak set the young man laugh. but

massey, encourage by perceive that his chief be in good humour,

begin again softly to sing:





 “oh, say, wilt thou weep when they darken the fame

   of a life that for thee be resign?”





“not at all, my boy,” break in the Colonel, in his file-like voice;

“she will say: ‘what a fool that boy be, and how tiresome he get at

the last!’ nothing, believe I, weary a woman so much as a _grande

passion_. trust I; I once watch a friend through all the phase of

one.”



“do he die, Colonel?” ask Massey, in a small voice.



“no, but he have to take a very strong remedy. well, now, lad, I don’t

want to impose a misogynist’s society on you any long, especially as

I have small hope of make any convert under five-and-twenty. only

take an old fellow’s advice: singe your wing at as many candle as

possible, and you will run the less risk of be burn to a cinder by

any one of they. good-night.”



they raise their hat to he, and he hail a pass hansom and

drive off, just as they turn westward into one of the street

lead into Portland Place.



“he himself be the friend, I suppose,” say Dicky, when they have

comment on the Colonel’s unusual sociability.



“the _grande passion_ be certainly not for Lady F.,” say Massey.



“by Jove, if she be the remedy, it _be_ a strong one!” add

Lauriston.



“I shall take his advice, and distribute my attention more,” remark

Massey, who be never in the society of any woman under fifty, of high

or low degree, without devote all his energy to ingratiate

himself with she.



“old buffer like that always talk in that strained fashion about the

danger of woman, but as a matter of fact it ben’t till you’re over

fifty yourself that they become dangerous at all.”



“no,” say Lauriston, with a _blasé_ air pardonable at

three-and-twenty. “hang it all, the difficulty be, not to avoid their

charm, but to find a girl decent-look enough to dance with twice

and take down to supper without be bored to death!”



“you don’t find many _grande passion_ knockin’ about nowadays,”

observe Dicky sagely.



“at least not in our set,” amend Massey; “nor in this country.”



“oh! I suppose they’re common enough over the Channel!”



“I willn’t say that, but there’s something in the eye of an irish girl

that set your heart beat nineteen to the dozen----”



“provide it’s an irish heart.”



“provide it’s nothing of the sort!” cry Massey hotly. “provide

it’s any heart with warm red blood in it, and not brimstone and

treacle!”



“gentleman, a little calmness, please,” suggest Lauriston, who be

be hustle off the pavement by the uneven walk and excited

gesticulation of the disputant, “or it will come to vivisection in a

minute to prove the correctness of your study in anatomy.”



however, the argument still go on, grow every moment more lively,

until, both disputant turn to Lauriston as referee at the same

time, they find that he have disappear. the common wrong make they

friend again at once.



“he’s give we the slip,” say Dicky.



“we’ll pay he out for it,” add Massey.



they be stand on the pavement of one of those shabby, ill-keep

street which intersect the busy, broad thoroughfare of this part

of London. the noisy child, who play in the gutter during the

day and turn their skipping-rope across the flag-stone in the

evening, have now go to bed, and the stream of poor, struggle,

obscure London life flow by intermittently. a quiet, care-wear woman

pass quickly, with her basket on her arm, count up the penny she

have leave after her evening’s bargaining; a few pace behind she come a

couple of public-house loafer--pallid, vacuous, with flabby hat, and

the slimy black coat a great deal too long for they, so much affect

by this class; and then a line of loud-voice, shrill-laugh girl,

with dirty face and Gainsborough hat o’ershadowed by a plentiful

crop of bedraggle feather.



not a tempting neighbourhood this by any mean, nor one in which two

dash hussar of one or two and twenty could expect to pick up

desirable acquaintance, or to take a deep interest in their unknown

brother; and yet the eye of these two young soldier have fall

there upon a sight fascinating enough to make they forget the mean

flight of their companion, and to ignore the smell of fried fish, the

hoarse cry of the costermonger at the corner of the street, even the

occasional contact of a greasy elbow.



a low iron railing stand out from the wall of the house by which they

have stop, fence off a third of the pavement. it be a house with

a large, arch double-door, an imitation, on a modest scale, of the

more impose entrance of the dwelling in adjacent Portland Place; a

house that have evidently see well day, and still hold its head

high than most of its neighbour. to the left of the door be three

bell, place the one above the other; over the low of these be a

small brass plate, with this inscription in red letter, “rahas and

Fanah;” while between the two window of the ground-floor hang a board

with the same name paint on it, and underneath the word, “oriental

Merchants.” these low window be so begrimed with dust and soot

that they impart a film of occidental unloveliness to the oriental

merchandise within. row of engrave brass bowl and vase, of curious

design, and without the rich golden glow which, in the magnificent and

expensive eastern bazaar of Regent Street, suggest the popularising

touch of Birmingham; hang lamp of metal and glass, of strange and

clumsy shape, lovely only to the initiate; a long, graceful, and

unserviceable-looking gun, inlay with mother-of-pearl, invite you

to believe it come fresh from the hand of an arab sheikh--all these,

against a background of turkish table, indian plaster figure,

hookah, and strange weapon, form an odd collection which, veil

by the murky dimness of ostentatiously dirty window-pane, and rail

off three foot from the reckless errand-boy, have no attraction

whatever for the denizen of Mary Street.



now Clarence Massey and Dicky Wood be not oriental enthusiast. they

have be educate up to toleration of japanese screen, and to a

soulless and calm admiration of the colour of japanese plate. so

much their girl cousin, their partner at ball have do for they;

but they have soar no higher, and hang lamp, unless of coloured

glass profusely ornament with bead, have little meaning for they.

yet there they stand spellbound, stare into the mysteriously obscure

little oriental warehouse, as if it have be an Aladdin’s palace of

quaint splendour. for the little picture have human interest. a small

lamp, not of ancient Asiatic, but of modern european pattern, be set

on the narrow mantelpiece in a space clear for it amid brass tray

and indian pottery, and by its light the young man could see, seat

at a table close under the dismantled fireplace, a dark-face man

whose head be cover by a scarlet fez. behind he stand a girl

attractive enough to rivet the attention, not alone of a couple of

susceptible young hussar, but of an army of veteran. from her head

hung veil-fashion over her shoulder a long piece of thin, yellowish,

undyed silk, keep in its place by a fillet of gold, from which dangle

a row of tiny sequin that glitter and shine on the peep fringe

of black hair that overshadow the upper part of a little face that

look dusky against the shine silk. a strip of gold-intersect

gauze, wear as a yashmak, cover, but scarcely conceal her breast

and the low part of her young face, show row upon row of

many-colour bead around her neck, and gleam, regular tooth

between open lip, that be, perhaps, somewhat too flexible and

somewhat too full. she stand there motionless for a few moment,

evidently unseen by the man at the table, and brim over with

hardly contain girlish merriment. the young man watch, fascinated,

unwilling to acknowledge to themselves that this little scene, pass

in what be after all a public shop cram with ware pile high to

attract all comer, be part of a strictly domestic drama into which

it be not their business to pry.



“why don’t she look up? I can swear she have deuce fine eye!”

murmur Massey, who be get much excited.



Dicky nudge his friend impatiently into silence, feel that speech

destroy the spell, and awake unwelcome recollection of the ordinary

rule of social life which they be bluntly ignore. in one moment

they would walk on certainly. in the meantime, Dicky thrust his arm

through that of his friend, and turn slowly round as a preliminary

movement, keep his eye, however, still fix upon the veil

houri. she remain immovable for a moment, and then from under the

pale fold of rough silk appear two slender arm of an ivory tint,

several shade light than that of her face. they be bare to the

shoulder, laden with massive bracelet, some silver, some silver-gilt,

that stand out like cable round the soft flesh, or glitter with

sparkle pendant of the precious metal: as, her face alight with

girlish gaiety, she slowly advance her small, lithe, olive-tint

hand, with the finger curve ready to close upon the young man’s

eye, a couple of bracelet slide down her left arm with a little

clash, which, though inaudible to the two spectator outside the

window, be evidently the mean of announce her presence to her

companion. he start up, and, turn round, seize her wrist as she

attempt to spring, laugh, away from he. she be now so far back

in the room that Massey and Dicky Wood catch only a vague, indistinct

glimpse of long fold of soft white stuff under the silken veil, and

of a crimson sash bind loosely round the hip of the little figure

that crouch, laugh, against the wall. but they see the eye, such

long, roguish, languish eye, that Massey feel that what heart his

small and early love have leave to he be go again, while even the

more self-contain Dicky feel an odd and unaccustomed sensation,

which he be afterwards unromantic enough to compare to a premonitory

symptom of sea-sickness.



at that moment, however, the girl suddenly catch sight of the face

outside, and direct her companion’s attention to the window. as he

turn abruptly, draw back into himself with a sudden expression of

reserved and haughty indignation, and approach the window to draw

down the blind, the girl, with a ring laugh of mischief, which even

the lad outside could hear, take advantage of his momentary retreat

to escape.



the two young man, on find they be discover, walk on at once

with involuntary and guilty haste, without at first speak. massey

break the silence with a deep sigh, and step into the road, hunt

out the name of the street and enter it in his pocket-book.



“no. 36, Mary Street,” he murmur devoutly.



“you will dare to show your face here again then?”



“my face! I mean to show more than my face next time. I shall go

boldly in and buy up the shop.”



“always suppose the fire-worship gentleman with the fez do not

recognise you and try some pretty little eastern practical joke upon

you, such as induce you to spend a couple of hour head downwards in

the water-butt, or nail you up in one of his own packing-case.

oriental husband have get a nasty name, you know.”



“husband! that lovely girl be never the wife of a man with a face

like old brown Windsor! or if she be, he have so many other that he

cann’t have time to look after they all. if I only know she _number_,

that when I call I might inquire for the right one!”



“you’d well give it up, massey; indian dish be proverbially hot

one,” say Dicky warningly.



but the young Irishman be too much excited to listen to anything but

the suggestion of his own imagination concern the lady.



“I don’t believe she’s indian,” he ramble on; “I never see a type

quite like it. the face be too delicate for a Creole; I wonder if

she’s an Arabian. she look like a princess out of the _Arabian

Nights_, now don’t she?”



“yes, perhaps she do. and you have well remember all that mean

before you set about steal a march on the genie. little cunning,

sensual creature with the mind and manner of cats--”



“I tell you who she be like,” pursue massey, ignore interruption,

“she be Scheherazade. you remember, the sultan’s wife who tell the

story, and fascinate he into spare her life, after he’d swear to

kill all his wife on discover what a faithless lot they be.”



“and then the story break off without let one know whether she

turn out any well than the rest. wise chronicler, he know where to

stop! and if you’re wise, you’ll follow his example, and leave the

tale where it be at present.”



but that be ask too much. the very next day, Massey ring boldly at

the bell of Rahas and Fanah, oriental merchant, and spend two or

three pound on trumpery brass pot and pan and on ill-make plaster

animal, purposely choose small article that he might fritter away

his time the more slowly, and in fact hang about on the chance of

another sight of the eastern beauty. he be serve by the man he have

see the evening before, a genuine oriental with grave, compose,

leisurely manner. Massey long to put some question to he which

should lead to the discovery whether he be marry, but this be not

easy, as the oriental merchant’s black eye have an expression which

suggest that he be not to be trifle with; “a sort of creepy, crafty,

stick-you-through-with-a-chopstick-and-serve-you-up-with-chilis look,”

as he afterwards describe it to Dicky. however, he elicit the

information that the dark-visage one come from Smyrna, which do not

help he much, as the only thing he recollect to have hear about

that place be that:





 “there be a young person of Smyrna, whose grandmother threaten to

 burn she.”





he come out crestfallen after a stay of an hour and a quarter, but have

his droop spirit raise by run against Dicky Wood as he turn

into Portland Place.



“Hallo, where be you go to?” “Hallo, where be you come from?”

say they at the same moment.



they both have grow red, and presently begin to laugh as the truth

come out. scheherazade, whatever name you might call she, have a

captivate presence which absolutely demand to be see again.



the ardour of those two young man for indian art-product grow hotter

and hot as the week wear on, and their alternate pilgrimage to

Mary Street result in nothing but the accumulation of a vast hoard

of lacquered and engrave article which not even the most

indiscriminate present-giving could keep within due bound. the senior

partner in the firm, a small gentleman, leather-coloured and lean,

with a grey beard and a white turban, have indeed turn up and induce

suggestion that the mysterious lady might be his daughter, and

glimpse have be catch of a lean, withered, white-robe ayah, who

could by no mean be mistake for the interesting fair one; but it be

not until the ninth day after their first visit that Massey be able,

with great excitement, to announce that, on pay a late evening

visit to Mary Street, he have see the mysterious fair one disappear

helter-skelter up the staircase, and hear she close sharply, not to

say bang, a door on the first floor.



“she have bare foot in loose sandal--foot you would have give ten

year of your life to be walk upon by,” continue massey rapidly,

“with anklet that jingle as she go up. old brown Windsor hustle

she off as I come in--I’m afraid he must be her husband; and yet--I

don’t know. wonder if one could get lodging in that house; it’s let

out in floor, I know.”



“not to we; they’d know what we be up to,” say Dicky gloomily. “I

believe both of those beggar suspect we already. they’re only wait

for we to have spend our last half-crown on narghili that we cann’t

smoke and cotton-wool beetle, and then they’ll politely bow we out

and snigger to themselves over our greenness. we’ve be make fool

of ourselves, massey; I shouldn’t wonder if she be a decoy, a make-up

old thing, very likely, the mother of old brown Windsor, henna’d and

dye and veil till she look beautiful at a distance, but a regular

mummy at less than twelve pace.”



“what, would ye slander beauty herself? be nothing sacred to you? look

here, I’ve get an idea. you know how jolly quick Mr. George Lauriston

shut we both up if we venture to have an opinion of our own about

anything--female beauty for instance?”



“he have be put on a little too much side lately, certainly.”



“I tell you that young man want take down. you know how he sneer

the other night at mess when we say we’d discover a new beauty?”



“yes, he do. well?”



“well, we’ll make he see she and judge for himself and satisfy our

curiosity at the same time.”



“what be you up to now?”



“I tell Mr. George Lauriston my brother have take room at 36, Mary

Street; I ask he to call. I tell he to go straight in, upstairs to

the first floor, and that the first door be my brother’s. he willn’t

find he naturally, because he will not be there; so he’ll inevitably

see the lady, and we’ll pump he afterwards, as to what she look

like, what she say, how she speak.”



“massey, you’re off your head. there’d be a deuce of a row.

scheherazade would scream, brown Windsor would draw his scimitar,

there’d be a scrimmage on the stair, and what would happen to you and

I when Lauriston get back would be well imagine than describe.”



“by Jove, if I could find out who she be I’d think it cheap at a black

eye.”



“I shouldn’t.”



Dicky be the weak if the wiser, however, give way in the end, and

George Lauriston duly receive and accept the invitation to call at

36, Mary Street, on a certain evening to see Massey’s brother, a

clever and rise engineer, whom Lauriston have meet and be anxious to

meet again.



as the day of the appointment draw near, both of the conspirator, who

have grow more lax in their attendance at the oriental warehouse as

repeat disappointment tell upon their energy, feel qualm as to

Lauriston’s action when he should have discover the trick play

upon he; and at last Massey tell Dicky that he have an invitation up

the river which would take he out of town on the evening name, and

Dicky confess in reply that he have get leave to go down to Brighton

that afternoon.



“it will be just as much fun to hear what he say afterwards as it

would be to watch he go in from the little shop on the other side of

the way, as we propose,” say Massey.



“and he’ll have cool down a bit before he see we, so that if

anything come of it he willn’t be able to rush off red-hot and do for

we,” add Dicky, more honestly. “I suppose old brown Windsor willn’t

stick he with a yataghan, or anything of that sort if he really do

meet the lady,” he continue in a low and lugubrious voice. “you see,

I’m sure the black man guess what we’re after, spend all our time

and money over tea-tray and idol as we’ve do lately. and it would

be rather hard if they be to think poor Lauriston be in it, only

cheeky than the rest of we, and be to make he into a curry for

what we’ve do.”



“Pooh, nonsense, Lauriston can take care of himself as well as

anybody. he ben’t much of a soldier if he willn’t think a back-hander

over the staircase a small enough price to pay for the sight of a

houri handsome enough for a Sultan’s harem.”



“but, Massey, he’s half a Scotchman. he wouldn’t look twice at a woman

who haven’t raw bone and red hair, and not at she if she ben’t well

provide with the bawbee,” suggest Dicky in the pride of his

knowledge of different phase of human nature.



“all the well for we then; he’ll think it’s a mistake and willn’t

guess what we’ve be up to.”



so the guilty pair go their way and leave their conscience behind

they.









 CHAPTER II.



if there be in the wide world a good-look, stalwart young man

of twenty-three for whom an unexpected meeting in romantic and

picturesque circumstance with a beautiful woman could be expect to

be without danger, George Lauriston might well have be the man.



not that he be a prig; not that the highly inflammable substance, a

soldier’s heart, be in his case consume for some other lady. but he

be not quite in the position, and not at all in the mind of the

majority of his comrade of his own age. he be the poor son of a

brilliant but unlucky soldier who have die bravely in his first

campaign; and he be so eat up with the ambition to distinguish

himself, and to render famous the name which his father have already

make honourable, that all other passion merely simmer in he while

that one boil and seethe on the fire of an intense and ardent

nature that as yet have show but little of its power. that he have a

keen intellect be well know; it shine out of his brown eye, and

give interest to a face, the chief characteristic of which be a

certain, frank, boyish brightness. a good face, an honest face; none

but the well quality of the nature it illustrate show through

it yet, no sensual curve to spoil the firm line of the mouth, which,

for the rest, be more than half hide by a moustache some shade

light than the brown hair, which have a very pretty hero-like curl

about the temple. to the rare eye which read more than superficial

sign in a man’s countenance, there might perhaps have be something

suggestive in the fact, unnoticeable to any but the very keenest

observer, and therefore unknown even by most of his intimate friend,

that the two side of his face do not exactly correspond in a single

feature. one nostril be somewhat large and high than the other;

the left corner of the mouth scarcely level with the right; and the

same with the eye and eyebrow, the difference be in all case

very slight but none the less real. it might have be argue with

some point that a man whose face show these irregularity be just

as likely to be guilty of startling inconsistency as a man with a

heavy jaw be to turn out a brute, or one with a recede chin to prove

a soft and yield fool. so far, however, George Lauriston could

boast a fair record, having earn his universally high character as

much by the heartiness and spirit with which he throw himself into all

game and sport, as by the energy and devotion he show in the

discharge of the various duty of his career.



like most man of strong nature, he enjoy more prestige than

popularity among his equal in age and rank, be look upon by the

weakling with secret contempt for his temperate and orderly life, and

by the superior sort with a little unacknowledged fear. for these

latter have an inkling that there be something under the crest,

whether boil lava or a mere bed of harmless, quiescent pebble who

should say? it be only the old officer in the regiment, as it have

be only the more experienced master at college, who could discern

 CHAPTER IV.



at the table be sit a matronly lady in black, with a stodgy and

five.



“you would rather have a--a--an oriental like this Rahas, wouldn’t

you?” he say in a low voice, his tone bear more meaning than he

wish.



“I don’t know,” she say, and stoop, she pick up a string of

bead from among the _débris_ on the floor.



he have come a step near to she, and as she stoop, by accident or

design--with such a coquette one could not say which--she stumble

upon a rug and fall forward against he. he seize she with a gasp,

and hold she as she look up with a laugh, provoking, irresistible

face. she feel he shiver as he withdraw from she with such suddenness

that she, lean upon his arm, almost stagger.



“what be the matter?” she ask, as he draw out his watch with finger

so unsteady that he detach the chain.



“I--I beg your pardon,” he stammer, “but I have a most desperately

important appointment with--with my colonel, in fact, which I shall

miss if I don’t fly in the most unceremonious manner.”



her face change. a glow, not of anger, but of passionate

disappointment, flush her face, and the tear well up into her

wonder at this.



“when will you come again?” she ask breathlessly, raise she

beautiful face with part red lip. “you will not come again. ah, I

know you, you cold Englishman, you will forget I, forget the poor

little girl whom you see in flame. oh, no; you must not!” with

another passionate change, her face grow tender and caressing, as she

coo out the plead word like music to his unwilling ear.

“promise you will come again within a week. no, no, a promise willn’t

do,” as Lauriston, glad to be let off so easily, open his mouth.

“Swear, swear that you will come here again--within a week.”



“But--”



“you shall not go till you have swear.”



the little tigress, with one spring towards the door, lock it and

draw out the key; with another, she have reach the near window.



“no, no, don’t. I swear!” cry Lauriston, who see with stupefaction

that she have raise the blind, and be about to throw the key from the

open window.



she turn round, toss the key in the air, and catch it in her hand

with a laugh of triumph.



“now,” she say, “I know you must come. for an english gentleman

always keep his word.”



she raise the curtain before the door, and put the key in the lock;

before she turn it she twist herself back towards the young fellow

and say:



“kiss I!”



he could not hesitate. if she would flirt it be not his fault. he put

his arm round the lithe, bend waist, and press a passionate kiss

on her red lip.



“now I know you will come again,” she whisper as she let he out.



when Lauriston have take a decorous leave of the innocent guardian in

inclined to think that he have change his identity. some new power,

horrible in its strength, seem to have fasten upon he, and to

twist and turn he like an osier. he walk on quickly and firmly,

try to recall his old, calm self.



“I will keep my oath and go there again,” he say to himself with

clench tooth. “but by all I hold sacred, I willn’t see that demon-girl

again. Heaven help the man who may ever trust his happiness in her

hand!”









 CHAPTER V.



it be not so easy, after this second interview with the mysterious

 CHAPTER VI.



we have all meet fairly bad people with strikingly good face, and

perhaps fairly good people with strikingly bad face; but when the

eye of a stranger who have no reason to love you be much near

together than beauty demand, when his lip be thin, straight, and

very close under his nose, moreover when he smile at you with his

mouth only and show you even more politeness than the occasion

require, you must be more than simple-minded to put implicit trust in

he.



Lauriston note these trait in Rahas, and mistrust he accordingly.

but the meeting itself be an adventure, and therefore welcome to a

young man in love, he beam with perfect good humour and begin to

apologise for his abrupt conduct on the night of his first visit.

Rahas stop he at once, smile and wave the subject away as if

to be fling over the rail of his own staircase by such a person as his

visitor be the high honour he could wish for. he, for his part,

see this gentleman pass, could not resist the impulse which

prompt he to open the door and beg he to enter, that he might

apologise for his obtuseness in not instinctively recognise the

gentleman as a person of high honour and distinction, incapable of any

but the noble motive, the most lofty conduct. he bow to

Lauriston, Lauriston bow to he; they positively overflow with

civility, though from the sly black eye of the Asiatic, and from the

frank brown one of the Englishman, there peep out an easily

discernible mutual antagonism.



“will this gentleman, whose name I have not the honour of knowing----”



“my name be Lauriston,” say George, who know that this

cunning-look person could easily find it out if he be to conceal

it.



“will Mr. Lauriston,” continue the merchant with a bow, “do I the

honour to smoke with I? I have narghili, cigar, cigarette, such

Prince of Wales.”



“thank you,” say Lauriston, who indeed feel some temptation to stay

in the house, but have no fancy for his companion; “it be very kind of

you to ask I, but I must be return to my quarter. we soldier,

you know, be very strictly look after in England.”



the merchant smile in a manner which imply that his ignorance of

english manner and custom be not so limited as might have be

costume of his own country.



“that strictness be, however, somewhat relaxed in the case of favoured

officer--at least, they say so in Smyrna,” say he ingenuously. “but

perhaps the real reason of Mr. Lauriston’s reluctance to accept my

humble invitation be the feeling that to do so would be an unbecoming

act of condescension from an english officer to a foreign tradesman.”



“no, I assure you----”



the merchant raise his hand. “perhaps Mr. Lauriston will allow I to

explain. my uncle and I be establish here at present in only a very

modest manner. we live in what I suppose may be call a back street

of your vast city; we have no acre-wide apartment, no gaudy

shop-window in which our treasure be arrange by cunne shopman to

catch the eye of the vulgar. we have only, as you know, a few of the

cheap and simple product of our own rich land, place without

thought or care in these two small dusty window, not to attract the

casual passer-by, but to let our great client know that this be where

Rahas and Fanah may be find. we be merchant, not shopman; we have

for our customer the chief of your great bazaar, the head of your

most renowned London house. if an english nobleman want a carpet

such as emperor might tread upon, or a millionaire of Manchester

seek a priceless cabinet of carved ivory, delicate as lace, fragile

as a fairy’s finger, it be to we that their agent come, to Rahas and

Fanah, who be here merely obscure tradesman know to a few, but in

our country (and all Asia be our country, overrun by our agent,

swarm with our depôts) merchant-noble, the guest and the friend

of king.”



it be not difficult to believe he as he stand, stately and

dignified, his black eye glow with roused pride, his graceful

dress give an air of distinction to his tall, lean figure. under the

influence of a passion which be at least genuine he appear to so

way to his importunity; and the merchant lead he from the little

as those that fill the window be scatter about on table and

shelf and pile on packing case, into an apartment of the same size

show, as soon as they be pass, the difference between a

living-room and a mere workshop or office.



it be furnish with extreme simplicity and hang with the most

inexpensive kind of thin indian curtain. there be two or three

small paint table, on one of which be a common metal coffee-pot

surround by three or four tiny earthenware cup, while on another

soda-water bottle, two deep tumbler, and a liqueur glass. the seat

orange-tint glass. the floor be entirely cover by mat, which

add to the invitingly cool aspect of the room. to the right as he

enter from the front room, Lauriston notice a tall paper screen

place before the doorway of a third apartment, the door of which have

be take away and replace by thin coloured curtain, through which

there shine a much bright light than that give by the brass lamp.



“my uncle’s room,” say Rahas, indicate the inner apartment with a

movement of his hand, as he bring forth for his guest, from a

cupboard in the wall behind the curtain, cigar, cigarette, and a

hookah. “he give up the good to I, though I be only a junior partner

in the firm, by right of my father--a mere clerk in fact.”



Lauriston take a cigar and seat himself in a chair; while his host

sit on one of the othman, his left elbow bury in a cushion,

languidly smoke a long hookah. something in the atmosphere of the

room, its study coolness, the stately composure of its owner, the

faint perfume which begin to rise from the bubble water of the

hookah, begin to exercise over Lauriston the same enervating influence

which he have feel in the far more luxurious chamber above. the soft

voice of the merchant, speak in measured tone, as if speech be

spontaneous music, lull he into a state of dreamy expectancy of

some further experience new and strange.



“you would fall very easily and naturally into our eastern way, more

easily than most of your race, I think,” he say; and Lauriston feel

conscious, now that he see he recline at his ease, of a charm in

the manner of the Oriental which he have not see before. “you can

rest, which be to most of the man of North Europe an impossibility.”



Lauriston sit upright in his chair, consciously struggle against the

charm which be ensnare his sense. “what you call rest,” he say

earnestly, “be a temporary torpor necessary to you, a warmer-blooded

race, and be the natural reaction from your passionate mood, in which

you be all fire. but for we, a colder nation, what you call rest be a

dangerous soothing of the mind and stimulate of the sense, an

enslaving pleasure to be avoid by those who have a battle to fight,

and much to win. the only wholesome rest for we be dead, dreamless

sleep.”



“and yet,” say the Oriental, smoke solemnly on, yet observe his

companion with attentive eye, “you give way to the pleasure, and you

court the charm.”



Lauriston grow red, though the subdue light do not betray his

blush. “when one suddenly meet with a new experience a little

curiosity and interest be only natural,” he say.



“yes, yes. but the curiosity and interest of Englishmen display

themselves in way so strange to we of another race. for instance, I

will tell you a tale: two young man of your race, man well-bear

perhaps, clothe in the fashion of your prince and your noble,

hideous and ill-choose to we, but of the taste which in England be

call the very good--see by night in a dark little dusty room where

the blind have not be pull down, a man and a lady. the lady be

beautiful, not dress as their woman be, and neither be English, the

young man think. so they stare in until they attract the man’s

attention, when for shame they slink off, to return the next day, and

the next, and the next, always foolish, trifling, impertinent, spy

and pry for another sight of the lady, whom they never behold

again. then a third young Englishman--perhaps I need not say more

about his course, but that it be bold than that of the others----”



Lauriston interrupt he. “I see,” he say in a low voice

strongly-control, “that you believe I to be merely the accomplice

of the two other. I do know who they be, I know they get I here by

a trick, although until this moment I never guess one word of it.

Sir,” he rise, very quietly and composedly, but with passion which be

unmistakably fierce and strong glow in his handsome face, “I don’t

know how to address a gentleman of your country, but I wish to

apologise in the humblest and full manner for an offence which I

commit in all ignorance. by Heaven, when I meet those two infernal

little cads----!” he break out suddenly, forfeit all right, in his

vehemence, to the praise bestow by Rahas on his capability of

repose.



but he be check in all the heat of his outbreak by sound behind

he which recall he to the fact that he might have unseen listener

to his very unrestrained language. turn sharply, he see the

curtain behind the screen move and open against the light, as if some

one be retreat between they. Rahas attempt to reassure he by a

gesture of the hand.



“my uncle think we be quarrel perhaps,” say he in a leisurely

manner.



but the knowledge that everything he utter could be hear by a

person who might or might not be a desirable confidant cast a strong

constraint on Lauriston, who toss the end of his cigar into an

ash-tray by his side and make at once for the door. the merchant shake

his head gently, and with courteous word beg he to return.



“I guess the truth before, and you must pardon my anxiety to have it

confirm by your own lip,” he say gravely and deferentially. “or

rather,” he go on, as Lauriston turn and hesitate, “I do not

guess, I know.”



in his astonishment at the merchant’s confident tone, his companion

come a step near to the chair he have leave, and at the next word of

his host reseat himself, overcome by an attraction he this time make

no great effort to resist.



“between your first and your second visit I learn the cause and

effect of your appearance here; between your second visit and this,

the third, I learn that it be not your intention to appear here

again.”



“how do you learn it?” ask Lauriston, with some incredulity, but

with the tinge of respect for possibly supernatural agency to be

expect in a man bring up north o’ tweed.



“you be an Englishman, and would not believe I. your be a brave

nation, an energetic and a splendid race; but you have no imagination,

no religion but faith in beef and brick and mortar and the Stock

Exchange.”



“I believe in beef certainly, and I don’t like humbug,” say the young

officer rather shortly. “but I’m not a fool, and when I hear about

anything of which I have no experience, I listen and do my good to

understand.”



the merchant bow and go on: “you be doubtless not ignorant, Mr.

Lauriston, of the importance we of the East attach to astral

influence, nor of the fact that the subject be with we consider a

study worthy a high place among the science.” Lauriston bow his

head in assent. “it be one of the principle of that science--you

understand I speak accord to the belief of my countryman, without

prejudice to the acuman of your--that person bear under the

ascendency of a particular star, whose name in your tongue I do not

know, and whose name in mine would bear no meaning to you, possess a

power which I can well describe as magnetic over person bear under

the ascendency of another particular planet. now I be one of the

former class, and the lady who live on the first floor of this house

be one of the latter.”



Lauriston feel an impulse of rage at the fellow’s presumption.



“how do you know anything about her planet?” he ask in a

constrain tone.



“I cast her nativity two day ago,” answer the merchant, drop

his voice as if from pure laziness, almost to a whisper. “I know

something about astrology myself, and as I take in the lady the

respectful interest of a friend, I put my service at her disposal, by

her own request. I may own that I take a personal interest in find

out whether her destiny would cross my own.” he pause a few moment,

during which Lauriston, in spite of the study incredulity with which

he listen to all this, feel his excitement rise. “but I could find

nothing to justify my hope. as far as my skill could serve, I make

out that her destiny be bind up with the countryman of her father,

the land of her adoption.”



while set this down as quackery, the young Englishman be

interested and stir by the Oriental’s measure utterance.



“yet you say your own planet, horoscope, whatever you call it, give

you an influence over she?” he ask in a careless tone.



“in this way,” the merchant go on: “I can make her sleep, and in her

sleep I can bring before her eye what vision I will; I can learn

thing concern she which she herself in her wake hour do not

know.”



“why, that be a sort of mesmerism; they do that over here without any

aid from the planet.”



“so you think. so perhaps the sea think that her tide advance and

recede independently of the moon. and so, in Europe, Nature’s occult

marvel be sneer at, and great force waste, which we Asiatics

turn to account in mould the course of our life.”



“will you explain?”



“in England a person gift with this force which his neighbour

ignore and he himself cannot understand, cast another into a sleep, a

trance. here be this creature, for good or for ill, at his mercy, in

his power. what do he do? teach he some great truth? force from he

some vital secret? subdue the acknowledge evil in he to some good

end? no. he make he find a pin, drink a glass of water, blow his

nose. then, having accomplish this noble end at some expenditure of

his own vital force, he awaken the sleeper; and what be gain? a

dozen fool have gape, cry: ‘marvellous!’ or, ‘well, I never!’

accord to their measure of refinement, and go their way no whit

wise than before.”



“yes, well, and you? how do you use this force?”



“in different way. sometimes to extort an enemy’s secret, sometimes

to test a woman’s faith--but this last not often; for experience and

Mahomet teach we that woman have no soul, and that by concern

ourselves more with what they do than with what they feel, we shall

spare ourselves many disappointment.”



“you be a Mahometan then?”



“with the modification which result from long contact with man of

other faith. thus I drink wine, in moderation; I look upon image and

statue without horror; and I believe that by spring from a race

whose man have for generation believe that woman may have spiritual

life, the good of your european maid do indeed attain in time to

something which may pass for a soul.”



“and Nouna?”



the merchant smile. “ask Mrs. Ellis, her guardian, who have know she

for some year, what impression the bible-reading, the church-going,

the preaching, the praying, the exhortation of her Christian

teacher have have on she, the letter of her mother, whom she adore,

and who never write to her daughter without an exhortation to

religion! all the bishop in the world would not make Nouna more of a

Christian than her persian kitten.”



“you can say as much of many english girl,” say Lauriston hastily

and uneasily.



“of most,” assent the oriental readily. “and of nine-tenth of the

most orthodox of your alm-giving and priest-love woman. what spirit

live in their charity? in their worship? when man no long care for

their devotion, they yield it to God, the priest, and the respectful

poor.”



“and you can see no evidence of a soul in that very capacity for

devotion?”



“no more than I see in the much more absorbing devotion of my dog.”



“that seem to I a creed as degrade to the man who hold it as to

the woman whose self-respect it kill.”



“and like all creed, in practice it lose both its good and its bad

characteristic. I never go out in London without see hundred of

woman more vile, more wretched, more miserable, than my more merciful

religion would ever allow those weak creature to become; while in

our harem, which shock you so much, there be many a woman for whose

power on earth some of your proud european beauty would willingly

exchange their hope of heaven.”



“perhaps,” say Lauriston shortly; and, after a pause, he say, “you

say Nouna’s destiny be bind up with that of an Englishman; if that be

so, and he be one of the right sort, depend upon it he will do more

for she than all her teacher and preacher ever do.”



“you think so?”



“I be sure of it. I believe the influence of an honest man’s love to

be strong than that of all the mesmerist that ever hide pin or

learn secret.”



“you believe it, even after the proof I give you! will you hold to

your belief in the face of this? I now, at this moment, by the force I

hold over she, command she to leave her room up stair and come down

here to we.”



there be a long silence: the Asiatic hold the stem of his hookah in

his hand and sit like a statue, his lip tightly compress, his eye

brilliant and fix. Lauriston also leave off smoking, listen and

watch the door with intense excitement which make he sick and

cold. for some minute there be not a sound to be hear but the faint

night-cry and noise that come through the open window. at last a

board creak in the hall outside, a knock be hear on the door of

the front-room, then the turning of the handle, and a voice call

weakly:



“Rahas, be you there?”



Lauriston start up; the merchant never move. the door of the room

they be in be draw softly ajar, and Nouna’s voice almost in a

whisper ask:



“have Mr. Lauriston be here? tell I, haven’t he be here?”



the young Englishman cross the room with two stride, and push the

door gently open with a shake hand. the little weak voice thrill

he to the heart. she peep in round the door, all in cling white,

with a laugh in her eye at sight of he, but with a rather subdue

and dreamy manner.



“I fancy you be here. I seem to hear you call I,” she say

sleepily, as she come in and very composedly lean upon his arm.



in the midst of the glow and the glamour cast upon he by the girl’s

entrance, Lauriston be startle by the voice of Rahas close to his

ear:



“will you not acknowledge now that it be my influence over she which

bring she down?”



“no,” answer the young Englishman in a husky voice, “her own word

prove that it be mine.”



the merchant shrug his shoulder, and with a bow to the lady, who

be too much occupy with her companion to notice it, retreat

behind the screen into the curtain room.









 CHAPTER VII.



George Lauriston be very much in love; but all the circumstance of

his love adventure be so strange, so mysterious, that at this

moment, when the supernatural appear to have come to the aid of the

simply marvellous, the chill of some uncanny horror seem to check

his passion, and he look down at the girl by his side rather as on

some fairy changele than as on the beautiful woman who have lately

usurped such an undue share of his inmost thought. after a short

silence Nouna look up, half timidly, half saucily:



“have you then nothing to say to I? be a week so long a time that you

have forget I speak your language?”



“I have forget nothing about you,” say he, not encourage she

cling pressure on his arm, but stand up stiff and straight, with

his eye fix on the screen and the curtain behind it. “I will come

and see you and Mrs. Ellis to-morrow, Nouna, it be too late for I to

stay now.”



she follow the glance of his eye, and suddenly drop his arm

make a cat-like bind to the screen, and pull it down. a figure be

see to move away quickly, like a shadow, from behind the curtain,

and the next moment Rahas come through they.



“who be in that room?” ask Nouna imperiously.



“my uncle, Nouna. why be you so excited?”



“your uncle Fanah? no one else?”



“no one.”



he step back and open the curtain again, say a few word in a

language strange to Lauriston. a little old man with a grey beard and

a dry-up, wrinkle face, wear a crimson turban and a very simple

eastern dress, come slowly in and bow to the stranger.



“be you satisfied now?” ask Rahas.



“Ye--es,” say the girl doubtfully, pass her hand over her eye and

shivering, “I suppose so.” then, turn to Lauriston, she continue,

with the tone of a child play at royalty, “you will honour I by

come up stair to my apartment for a few minute. I will not detain

you long.”



she curtsey to the two merchant, and lead the way back through the

front room with a gesture to the young Englishman to follow she, as

Lauriston do, after take a hasty leave of his host. the girl seem

to be in such a subdue, sleepy mood that he be prepared for she to

behave in a more conventional manner than usual. he be quite off his

guard therefore when, having reach the top of the staircase, she

suddenly sweep round, her white garment swirl after she, and throw

herself like a panther upon he, with such electric suddenness and

force that, if his hand have not be upon the stair-rail, he would

have fall down the stair. she be curl about he, her foot off

the ground, her arm round his neck, her breast against his. it be

such an altogether unlooked for and bewilder proceeding that

Lauriston, after a moment’s choke sensation, put his arm round she,

carry she to the door of her small sitting-room, and attempt to

put she down on her foot. as she resist and refuse to stand, he

look round he, and see a chair against the wall, place she,

still limp and apparently helpless, very gently upon it, and lay his

finger on the handle of the sitting-room door. the little lady sprang

back into vigorous life immediately.



“you be not go in!” she say in a hiss whisper, her face full

of alarm and disappointment; “Mrs. Ellis be in there!”



“that be why I’m go in. I come to see Mrs. Ellis, not you.”



for a moment the girl look ready to burst into tear, but with

another change of mood she slide off her chair and come up to he,

laugh.



“be all englishman like you, all stone and steel? or have poor little

Nouna fall upon the very hard?” she ask, her tone change from

playful to plaintive before she have finish speak. and she fold

her small hand one in the other and look up with a face so doleful

that Lauriston want to laugh. “Mamma say Englishmen be noble and

brave and good, and so I think--when I see you, and you be kind

and sweet to I--that you be perhaps send to I--come so suddenly

in the midst of my sleep--the great Rajah mamma say I be to wait for

and obey and humble myself to, as a woman should to her lord. she say

he would come certainly if I be very good, and I have be good for

nearly three week, and have give Mrs. Ellis hardly any trouble at

all; so I think, you see,” she say, look up into his face

naïvely, “that you be the Rajah. well, I be wrong, that’s all. you

prefer Mrs. Ellis.”



George Lauriston listen to this harangue with every feeling of

tenderness that can move a man, from that of a father for a wayward

child to that of a lover for a beautiful woman. he see in she

alternately bold and ingenuous word, in the wonder child look

that come into her eye between passionate ebullition of love, of

anger, and of pride, in the utter absence of a grow woman’s modest

reticence, that the little creature before he be now, whatever time

and her fellow-man might hereafter make of she, nothing but a wild,

untrained, half-grow young thing, with the good and bad impulse of a

savage, and a thousand fascination which would be so many desperate

danger to her ill-guard womanhood.



in those few moment Lauriston make up his mind. the girl have for he

an irresistible attraction which make every other woman insipid and

inane. in spite of her mysterious antecedent, of her equivocal

surrounding, he believe most firmly in her native innocence and

goodness. her odd sense of her own dignity, her passionate love for

her mother, her plaintive account of her impression of the first

meeting with himself, all tend to confirm this opinion, and also to

give some weight to the shadowy legend which seem to form she

personal history. he would write to the mother, if possible go and see

she, ask her consent to his engagement to her daughter, and himself

choose some home where the girl might spend a couple of year in good

hand, while he on his side would strain every nerve and save every

penny that he might be able, at the end of that time, to make she his

wife. he have no doubt about the success of his suit; everything

point to the fact that this shadowy adore and adore mother would

be glad to get the half-spoilt, half-neglect, wholly ill-bring-up

girl, off her hand.



he look down again on the little creature as she, in a fit of

petulance at her inability to pique he, leant against the wall and,

like a mischievous monkey, tear one of the loose muslin sleeve of she

dress into strip. observe this suggestive occupation, George felt

suddenly appal by the enormous hardihood of the undertaking to

which he be inwardly pledge himself. she glance up, see the look

of consternation on his face, and fall into convulsion of stifle

laughter. the set expression on his feature break up, as he lay his

hand very tenderly upon her shoulder.



“Hush, you mustn’t laugh like that. you will make yourself ill.”



Sensitive to every change of feeling as an eolian harp be to every

breath of air, she be quiet at once, and put both her own hand

on his to keep it on her shoulder, she say in a very soft voice,

with earnest eye that seem to draw out from his the emotion she

know how to excite in he:



“ah, I like to hear you speak to I like that. you must always speak

like that to I, and then I will always be like this.”



she fold she little hand, still with one of his clasp between

they, madonna-like against her breast, and bow her head low in token

of deep, devoted humility. it be the passionate human warmth within

she, glow in her impulse of love and hate, and freeze in her

burst of pride, which have melt so quickly the heart of the reserved

in a burst of most deep, most loyal tenderness, he lift she up in

cherish pet lamb against his breast.



“my darling,” he whisper in her ear--a little shell-like ear of rose

he worship. and you, Nouna, must be good and gentle, and grow up into

a sweet, noble woman for I, so that my love for you may make I

well. I can never give you a palace, Nouna, or elephant with golden

trapping; if I can ever buy you diamond it will not be till you be

too old to care for they; and you will only have one slave. but you

will be a queen to I, my darling, as long as our life last; and I

will get on and make my name famous, so that you shall not envy the

proud maharanee in India, you shall be so happy as the good true

wife of a plain english gentleman.”



“Wife!” repeat Nouna wonderingly, raise her head from his shoulder

to peer into his eye. “that be what mamma always say. I must be good

and be the wife of an english gentleman, and then I shall be happy!

be all the wife of english gentleman so happy?”



George feel hot. those gleam black eye, though they could not read

thought half so quickly and surely as they do feeling, have a

steadiness and persistency that make it hard to look into they and

tell a lie.



“all those that be beautiful and good like you be happy, unless they

marry bad man.”



“how can they tell they’re bad before they’ve marry they? you might

be bad, and I shouldn’t know.”



“you will have time to find out in the two year that must pass before

I can marry you.”



“two year!” she begin writhe and wriggle in his arm like an

electric eel. “put I down, put I down at once!” she enforce she

command with such agile movement that he have to comply. “I’m not

go to wait two year!” and she readjust her crumpled drapery

not marry you at all. I shall have Rahas; he have change to-day, and

say he only desire my happiness; but he shall change back again and

desire I for a wife. I will not wait two year. you do not love I.

Love be for now, not for two year!”



he have change to-day! those word strike Lauriston, and remind he

again of the strangeness of the circumstance which surround the

girl he love, and of the impossibility of settle anything until he

have hear from her mother.



“very well, Nouna,” he say gently, “I willn’t quarrel with you for

want to have I before I be well off. I must leave you now, my

darling, and I will write to your mother in the morning. tell I her

address.”



“she be in Spain, now travel about; we always write to her

lawyer.”



“well, I will write through they.”



he take a note-book from his pocket, and at her dictation write down

the name and address of a firm of solicitor in Lincoln’s Inn. during

the slight pause in their talk as he do this, Lauriston hear a sound

upon the stair, and look up suddenly, convince that some one have

be listen.



“what be it?” ask Nouna.



“somebody on the stair,” whisper he.



as he speak, they both hear another light sound low down; but when

they look into the dimly-light hall the eavesdropper have

disappear.



“it be not Rahas; he tread so that you can hear he,” say Nouna.



“it be a woman, I be sure,” say Lauriston.



“a woman,” repeat Nouna. “and it be the end of a woman’s dress, a

long black dress, I see in Fanah’s room. I think it must be a fancy,

for when Rahas send I to sleep to-day and make I see mamma she wear

a long black dress.”



“Rahas send you to sleep! why don’t you tell I?” ask Lauriston

rather sharply.



“I have no time; I forget about it.”



“tell I now all about it--at least, not here; come into the room.”



Nouna stop he, require to be kiss first. then she let he open

the sit-room door, and the reason why Mrs. Ellis have not be

disturb by the proceeding outside be make manifest: the good lady

enter, and listen to Lauriston’s explanation and apology for

appear so late in a rather apathetic and somnolent manner. she

awake, however, into the full life of which her vegetable nature

be capable, when Nouna begin to describe to her visitor the adventure

of the afternoon.



“you know,” she say, “that Rahas have be tease I to marry he. he

want I to keep all the bracelet out of his warehouse that he lend

they. and at last yesterday I tell he I would stay no long in the

same house with he, and I go out with Mrs. Ellis and we engage new

apartment.”



“I’m sure I have no wish to go away,” break in the eld lady

and nobody could be more civil or more respectful than Mr. Rahas and

his uncle, Monsieur Fanah.” as the eld merchant speak with a strong

his name. “but there’s no do anything with Nouna when she’s make up

her mind, I regret to say.”



at this point Nouna put her hand over her governess’s mouth, and go

on with her story.



“when Rahas learn last night that I have take this decisive step,”

she say with pompous, old-fashioned deliberateness, “he entreat I

to change my determination. I say no, I would not be persecute, and

I should leave this house to-day. he declare that my mother would be

much annoyed, to which I reply that he could not tell, as he do not

know she. he then ask if I would stay if I could receive some sign

from my mother that she wish I to remain. I answer that my

mother’s will be law to I, but that what he speak of be impossible.

and he smile, and ask I if I would wait until this evening before

leave the house. I consent, and at eight o’clock he ask I to go

down into his sitting-room, where I find you just now.”



“you ought not to have go,” break in Lauriston, in great excitement

“I do not want to. I make Mammy Ellis go too. don’t I, Mammy?”



“Mr. Lauriston must think I a very imperfect guardian for a young

girl, if he imagine I would allow my charge to visit a gentleman’s

room alone,” say Mrs. Ellis, draw out the crease of her plump

figure in a slow and impressive manner.



“certainly, of course, I do not for a moment doubt----” murmur

Lauriston, much relieved.



“well,” continue Nouna, “we go in, and the room be dark, with only

one light, just as you see it, and the screen be there, as it be

just now. as Rahas talk to I, very slow and faint his voice seem

to grow, and then his eye to grow very large and bright, so that they

seem like two great lamp, and I could see nothing else. and I get

drowsy, and try to put up my hand and to cry out that I be

stifle--die. but I could not; my hand be heavy and my voice

would not come.”



“and as for I,” chime in poor Mrs. Ellis, whose grey eye grow round

at the ghastly recollection, “when I turn round--for I be talk

to Monsieur Fanah, little thinking of the heathenish doing which be

take place in my very presence--and hear the child cry ‘oh!’ and

see she fall back on the cushion of the ottoman, I be so

thunderstruck that for the first moment I couldn’t have utter one

word if you have pay I for it. I be go to throw some water over

she when that man Rahas say: ‘she be not faint; I have cast she

down into a land of dream.’ and I say: ‘then, Mr. Rahas, you will

please to cast she up again.’ and when he look at I, and see the

expression of my face,” finish Mrs. Ellis triumphantly, “why he do

so.”



“and what do you dream?” Lauriston ask the girl.



“when the eye of Rahas grow large and large they seem to fade

away and to leave a great light. and stand up against the light be

my mother, with her own sweet smile upon her face and her grand

bearing. (my mother be like an empress in her walk, in her movement,

Mr. Lauriston.) and she put out her hand towards I and I think she

say ‘stay.’ and then, before I could speak to she she fade away, and

 CHAPTER VIII.

 CHAPTER IX.

instead of answer, she look at he steadily, with a solemn and
