know the reason of her unexpected coming, her appearance be so

comical that George could not help smile as he look at she. she

wear again the shot silk frock in which he have see she on his second

visit to Mary Street; a deep, purple embroider fez make a Romeo-like

cover for her short and curly dark hair; while a sop throw to

conventionality in the shape of a small black-bead mantle only

bring into great prominence the eccentricity it be mean to

disguise. she have either forget or not think it necessary to

exchange her open-work pink silk stocking and embroider scarlet

morocco slipper for foot-gear less startling and picturesque; her

glove, if she have wear any, she have long ago throw aside, and George

could not help acknowledge, as he look at she, that it would need

an intelligence strong than poor Massey’s to discover in this

remarkable guise the carefully bring-up young english lady whom

alone his code teach he to respect. as this thought come into his

mind, George’s expression change, and grow gloomy and sad. the young

girl be still watch he narrowly.



“be you often--so?” she ask, with a pause before the last word, and

a mysterious emphasis upon it.



“what do you mean, Nouna?”



“so full of anger that your face grow all white and grey, and your

mouth like a straight line, and you look as if you would kill the

person that offend you.”



and she shudder and draw away from he again. George take one of her

hand very gently in his.



“no, Nouna, I be very seldom angry like that. it be only when any one

do something which seem to I very wrong.”



“oh.” this explanation do not seem so re-assure to the young lady

as it ought to have be. “you be listen at the door all the time

then?” she say, after a pause, not so much in fear as in timid

respect.



“no,” say he vehemently, grow hot at the suggestion. “gentleman

don’t listen at door.”



“don’t they?” say she incredulously. “why not?”



“it be mean, sneaking, bad form altogether.”



“be it? then how do you find out thing? ah, you would pay a servant,

perhaps, to do all that for you?”



George grow scarlet and draw his hand away from she, half in

indignation, half in horror.



“Nouna!” he exclaim, “where on earth do you pick up these awful

idea? to pay a servant to play the spy be the most rascally thing a

man could do! a man who do it would deserve to be kick.”



“would he? then what would he do if he think his wife deceive he?

wouldn’t he mind?”



George spring up to his foot, and take a few turn about the room. he

be appal by this fearful perversion of mind, by this terrible

candour, and he think with a shudder of Rahas’ statement that the

woman of the East have no soul. how should he set to work to make she

see thing with his eye? he glance at she, and see that she have

change her attitude; curl her foot up under she, and lean she

head on her hand, she sit quite still, nothing move but her eye,

watch he. he come back, knelt before she, and look into her

face.



“in England, Nouna,” he say very gently, “when a man want a wife, he

choose a girl whom he believe to be so good, so true, so noble that

he couldn’t possibly think she would do anything that ben’t right.

and he love she with all his heart, and never think of anybody but

she, and spend all his time try to make she happy. so he never

think about such a dreadful thing as her deceive he, because, you

see, she couldn’t, unless she be very wicked, treat a man badly when

he be so good to she.”



“then what Sundran say be all wrong.”



“who be sundran?”



“my servant, my ayah. she say that englishman love other woman

besides their wife, who be only like the chief wife in our country,

and that Englishwomen be not so good as indian lady, because they

be not shut up.”



“sundran must be send away. she tell you falsehood,” say George

indignantly.



“she shall not be send away,” retort Nouna, flash out at once

into passion. “she love I. it be she who tell I of the land where

I be bear, who sing I my indian song, and tell I the tale of my

own country. she shall not go.”



George do not press the point, though he make an inward vow to remove

this most noxious influence as soon as he have authority over the

wayward creature before he.



“and don’t some one else love you, Nouna?” he ask reproachfully,

look into her flash eye; “some one whom you be treat very

cruelly this evening?”



the appeal melt she at once, or rather it turn the passion of

anger into a passion of affection. she throw her arm round his neck

and fervently kiss his mouth, nestle her red lip under his

moustache, and scratch his left ear fearfully with the beadwork on

her mantle.



“I be not cruel, I love you,” she say earnestly. “I come here

to-night because I could not live without see you again; only I

will not marry you; I have make up my mind to that.”



“but why, Nouna, why?” ask poor George in consternation.



“we be not of the same race; we should not be happy; Rahas be right.

if I make you angry, you would look as you do to-night, and you would

kill I.”



“but, Nouna, I be not a savage; I don’t get angry over little thing.

and I should never be angry with you.”



“I don’t know. you say you would not have I watch, you would trust

I. well, I would rather be watch, then I should feel safe. but if I

always do just as I feel, I should some day make you angry, I know. I

be not like your english girl--the girl at the school, who always

know what they be go to do. something come up here,”--and she put

her hand over her heart--“and then it mount up there,”--joining her

finger over her head--“and say, ‘Nouna, love; Nouna, hate; Nouna, be

sweet and gentle;’ or ‘Nouna, be proud and distant.’ and I go just as

the little voice guide. well, that be not English!”



“you be impulsive, darling, that’s all. if you love I truly, the

little voice will always tell you to do what I wish.”



“will it? but I be afraid. I tell you I would rather kill myself than

have you look at I as you look at the little curly-haired man

to-night. why do you hate he so?”



“when I come in he be try to kiss you.”



“but I would not have let he.”



“no, of course not. that be because you be good and true to I, whom

you love.”



“be it? I do not know it be that. I only know that he be small and

ugly, and I do not like he.”



“no; you must not like any man but I.”



“ah, then you have well shut I up.”



“well, so I will, my darling. I will shut you up in my heart so close

that you shall have no eye for any one but I.”



and with a great impulse of tenderness for the little dark-eyed thing

who be drink in new impression of life and moral with so much

solemn perplexity, he fling his arm round she and bury his head in

the fold of her dress.



“you will scratch your beautiful face,” say she solicitously,

remove the beaded mantle, and ruffle up his hair with light

finger. “how can Rahas say you be not handsome? you be like Brahma

himself when he ride in his sun-chariot!” she say with loyal

intention if with confused lore.



the name of Rahas, use thus for the second time, rouse George from

the intoxicate oblivion of outside thing into which this unexpected

interview with the girl he love have throw he.



“Rahas!” he repeat, raise his head sharply. “you haven’t see he

again to-day?”



“now you be go to be angry,” exclaim the girl shrink.



“no, my darling, I be not,” say George in a most gentle tone. “but if

I be to watch over and protect you, I must keep you out of the way of

man like Rahas. when do you see he? what do he say to you?”



“well, I see he this evening, just before I come here. Mrs. Ellis and

I have just finish dinner. I have be very quiet and good all day,

write a long letter to mamma, tell she how handsome you be, and

how I would never look with love on the face of any other man, if only

she would give I her permission to love you. and I be tired of

sit still, and the air be hot, and Mammy Ellis be sleepy. so I

open the door, and she say there be a draught, and I must shut it.

and I could not bear the heat, so I do shut it, with myself outside.

and I go into the next room--the one where I have pull down the

hanging; and I be so lonely and sad and weary that I be sorry I have

pull they down, and I begin to cry and try to nail up the long

trail of silk to the wall again; and when I find I could not, I sit

down and cry again; and then I look up and I see Rahas in the room

watch I like a tiger. and I spring up; but he come to I with his

eye shine, and fall at my foot and tell I a lot of strange thing

that I forget.”



“what thing? what do he tell you?” ask George, try to keep

calm.



“must I remember they? he frighten I; I do not want to remember

they.”



“try, my darling.”



“he say he love I, and that I must love he, for the planet say

so, and have give he an influence over I which I could not resist.

he say he have try to conquer himself, and have consent to give I

up; but his love be too strong, and I must forget you--that you be

hard and cold. when he say that I fly into a passion, and tell he I

hate he and should marry you. and I throw open the window and tell

he if he come near I I would shriek with all my strength. and so he

have to grow quieter; and then he say he know strange thing about I

I myself do not know, and that you never mean to marry I, that you

look upon I as a little girl to play with, and would marry a staid

english lady. and I burst out cry again, and say that would make

no difference to he, for I should go away and perhaps drown myself.

then he be very quiet for a long time, and he presently speak in oh!

such a low voice, with a smile on his face that be not sweet and

kind, but horrible. he tell I if I want you to marry I I have

well go to your room at once and tell you not to forget I love

you, or else that you would see some other lady and perhaps marry she

before I could see you again. so I spring up at once, and he tell I

where you live, and I slip into my own room and put on my mantle,

and tell Sundran not to say where I be go, and I would be back

soon. then Rahas put I into a cab, and tell I at the last to be sure

and wait till I see you. and I thank he and say, ‘be sure I will.’

and so I come; and it be a long way, and I be tired. why do you look

like that? why be you angry? do you not want I to come? Rahas say

you would be glad.”



“the infernal scoundrel!” burst out George, who have be listen to

this recital in almost incredulous horror.



Nouna get on to her foot, and look at he with a puzzled inquiring

face.



“do he know you would be angry then?” she ask in a low voice. “I

remember he say I should be less proud when I come back.”



with a strong effort George control himself, lest an incautious

word should give any inkling of the rascal’s meaning to the girl’s

mind. he draw forward an arm-chair and invite she to take it with the

manner he would have use to a princess. in seat herself she hold

up her arm towards he, but he would not touch she. he sit down

gravely a little way off as he say:



“you will not go back to Mary Street at all, Nouna.”



“no? shall I stay here with you?”



“not here, dear. these be bachelor’s quarter. but you will stay

after Friday in apartment that I shall take for you.”



“why after Friday?”



“because I cannot marry you before then.”



“marry I! you say you be not go to marry I for two year!”



“you see I’ve change my mind.”



“since last night?”



“since an hour ago, since I find you here.”



she spring up and fling her arm about he, with kiss, and caress,

and incoherent word.



“then you be not angry with I for come. oh, I’m so glad, I’m so

glad I come. I don’t know what to do, I’m so happy. it seem so

dreadful to have to wait two year, two year always away from you.

for I never feel like this before, as if my heart would break, or

would burn my breast if I be away from you. that be love, ben’t it?

kiss I, kiss I, don’t be so cold. don’t you love I? why be you

go to marry I if you do not love I?”



she push herself suddenly away from he, keep her hand on his

shoulder and devour his face with an eager scrutiny. his dark eye

be very bright, and his skin, burn red and brown like that of most

young englishman in summer time, be a deep colour than ever with

excitement. but his forehead be pucker into line and wrinkle, and

his mouth be close in a firm straight line, a fact which Nouna

discover for herself by brush up his moustache with a quick and

unexpected movement.



“you be think!” she cry indignantly. “when I tell you of my love

you be full of nothing but your thought. when I be your wife I will

not let you think.”



this last passionate sentence strike George with the ominous force of

a prophecy. he get up and lift the girl playfully right above his

head, however, while he speak in grave tone, the tenderness of which

be unmistakable. “when two people love each other, little one, one of

the two at least always have to think. and when you be my wife you

will have to let I do as I please, just as now you have to let I

hold you in the air until it be my good pleasure to put you down.”



but as he speak the little creature, who have be try in vain with

her weak finger to undo the clasp of his strong one on her waist,

suddenly cease to struggle and lie limp and heavy on his hand, her

head and limb hang loose, and her cap fall to the ground.

George let she down and place she on the sofa in consternation,

blame himself for ignore the fragility of the tiny thing. there

she lie just as he place she, as still as the dead. no soon,

however, have he rush into his bedroom, return with a glass of

water which he begin nervously to sprinkle on she still face, than she

open her eye with a sly and elfish delight, and begin to curl up

with mischievous laughter. George fall back with a sick feeling which

be not all relief at find she be less fragile than he have

suppose. he have challenge she to take his playful action as an

allegory, and she have have the wit to accept and continue it. when

their two will should clash she would obtain by fraud what she could

not get by force. it be at least a fair warning. he be angry with

she and he get up from his knee without speak, without look at

the laugh girl. Nouna understand, and in a moment all merriment have

die from her face; she be cling to his arm, entreat he

passionately to forgive she; she be a wicked, ungrateful girl; she

have only mean to tease he, to see if he would mind if she be ill;

she would obey he, she would do whatever he wish she to do; she

would throw herself out of the window if he would not turn and kiss

she.



so he turn, of course, and the kiss of peace be give; but George

have have a chill in the height of his passion, and even while he pass

his hand over her soft hair and make she pretty, low-voice

love-speech, his mind be full of practical matter concern she

lodge for the three night to be pass before he could possibly

marry she, and other detail connect with this step.



“where be this school you live at before you come to London with

Mrs. Ellis, Nouna?” he ask suddenly.



“School! it ben’t quite a school. there be only six of we, and we

all have room apart and our own servant,” say Nouna.



“well, but where be it?”



“it be at Clifton. but why--”



“Clifton! that’s no use,” say George to himself. then he continue

aloud, “now, Nouna, will you be a good child and stay quietly where I

take you to-night?”



“yes,” say she, nod like a child, “if you come too.”



“I’ll take you there to-night, and I’ll come and see you quite early

in the morning. it be to the house of an old servant in our family who

now let part of her house in apartment. she will be very kind to

you, I know.”



“but you willn’t leave I there all alone--without Sundran or anybody?”



“I willn’t leave you until I see you be quite happy and comfortable

there, and if you don’t like the place and the people I willn’t leave

you there at all.”



without give her time for further objection, George bring she a

comb, which she kiss because it be his and proceed to pass

through her short, thick hair in a very helpless and unaccustomed

fashion; at last, come to a decided knot, she stamp her foot and,

leave the comb in her hair, present her head to George, who place

she again in the armchair and reverently and laboriously set to work

on the soft curly tangle. he grow very hot over the occupation, which

be new to he, and begin to understand why hairdresser be generally

of the abler sex. by the time he have reduce the pretty wavy hair to

order, and admire its soft silkiness by the light of the candle he

have set burning, Nouna have add to his difficulty by fall, like

a tired kitten, fast asleep. he call to she gently two or three

time, and be at last force to come to the conclusion that she do

not mean to wake up. after a moment’s reflection he resolve to take

advantage of the circumstance. it be get very late, and if he

be to insist on rouse she, she might have another little scene in

store for he before she would consent to go. so he put on her cap,

pick up her glove and put they in his pocket, and lift she in

his arm, wrap the beaded mantle about she and carry she down

stair, during which proceed she pat his cheek sleepily but

really seem only half awake. he pass nobody but the sentry, who

could scarcely conceal his surprise on find which of the young

officer it be who be engage in such an evident “lark.” he be just

in time to catch a train to Victoria, and until they arrive there

Nouna decline to wake up. outside the station, George get with his

sleepy charge into a hansom and, after give the driver an address in

a street at Brompton, occupy himself, as his companion remain

motionless except that as he prop she in the corner she promptly

fall back against he, in get her little hand into her glove. he

be very tenderly busy with the first, when a voice from the depth of

his shoulder surprise he.



“wrong hand!”



“Hallo! so you’re awake, be you, little one? come, lift up your head;

I want to put you to right before we get there.”



“no, no, I don’t want to get there,” say she, stretch up her arm

across his breast, “I want to drive about like this all night with

you.”



“but we cann’t do that, Nouna.”



“why not?”



“well, you’d catch cold--”



“no, I shouldn’t; I’m quite warm; feel I.”



“and--and it wouldn’t be consider right.”



“now you’re not go to talk as Mrs. Somers use to talk, be you?”

ask Nouna warningly. “because if you do I shall hate you just as I

use to hate she, and I shall want to get away from you just as I use

from she.”



“but, Nouna, some one must tell you what’s right and what’s wrong, and

who be to do it if you willn’t let I whom you say you love?”



“don’t say I only _say_ it,” cry she, nestle up to he with

plead reproach. “go on, go on lecture I; but keep your hand so

on my shoulder all the time.”



and his lecture have to die away into endear word, and when the

hansom stop, George find it difficult to resist the temptation,

urge upon he in a soft whisper by his companion, to tell the cabman

to “drive on.” but he nerve himself to a sense of duty and propriety,

jump out and rang the bell of a well-keep house, at the door of

which appear a neat servant who inform he that Miss Glass be at

home.



Miss Glass be a woman of five and forty, with an honest

fresh-coloured face, who insist on kiss George because she have

nurse he when he be a baby, and who willingly consent to do all

she could for his bride-elect. the eccentric appearance of that lady

when she be bring inside the house seem somewhat to shock her

idea of propriety; but when, after George have bid her good-night

and go to the door, the poor child run out after he and entreat

he not to leave she--she be so lonely--she have never be all by

herself among stranger before--Miss Glass put her arm round the girl

in very kindly fashion, and soothe she into some sort of despondent

and melancholy resignation.



“you’ll come early, willn’t you?” Nouna cry from the doorstep in

heart-break tone. “if you don’t come before ten I shall come to the

barrack after you.”



George assure she that he would come before breakfast, and drive off,

excite by the rapidity with which this important step of marriage be

force itself upon he. he have surprise himself lately by develop

an infinite capacity for do rash thing, but he be save by his

native obstinacy from the weakness of regret they; therefore,

although he acknowledge to himself that this headlong plunge into

matrimony be the rash act of all, and that his chance of domestic

happiness be about the same as if he have decide to unite himself

for life to a Cherokee squaw, he be resolve in dare-devil fashion to

stick to his colour and make the good of it, and this state of mind

leave he calm enough to think of a little act of kind consideration

towards poor Mrs. Ellis who, he know, must by this time be half crazy

with anxiety about her charge. so he drive to Mary Street, and after

satisfy the governess that Nouna be safe, though he decline, for

fear of Rahas, to give her address, he go down stair and knock at

the door of the oriental merchant’s apartment. the grey-haired Fanah

open it, however, and with a real or affected ignorance of English,

explain, chiefly by gesture and incoherent noise that his nephew

be “go away.” so that George, who be burn for some short and

sharp vengeance, he hardly know what, upon Rahas for his infamous

advice to Nouna, be force to retire with that praiseworthy wish

unsatisfie.



Scarcely, however, have the old merchant, with a low bow, close the

door of his apartment, when a little lamp, bear by a figure in

white, cast a feeble light upon the wall above, which shift rapidly

downward until it be flash in George’s eye by the bearer, who

prove to be no other than Nouna’s indian servant Sundran. the young

man start when he see the bronze-colour face peer up into his.

it be a most unprepossessing countenance, bear the impress of mean

passion and low cunning, which not even the brown dog’s eye, full of

affection and a certain sagacity, could redeem. the woman might have

be of any age between thirty-five and fifty, though the supple

agility of her movement seem to prove that the wrinkle and line

in her dark face be premature. she look up into Lauriston’s face

with eager anxiety.



“Missee, little missee, my mistress, where be she?” she ask in a

whisper.



“she’s all right, quite safe; I come to tell Mrs. Ellis so.”



“but I want to see she, she not sleep till I come to she and sing and

tell she the old story. take I to she, sahib, take I, and Sundran

love you very dear.”



“I cann’t do that, Sundran; she be a long way from here. but she be

quite safe. I be go to marry she, so you may be quite sure she be

safe. Mrs. Ellis trust I, so you can.”



“but, sahib, Missee Ellis not know she so long as I. I come with she

from her country with the Mammee Countess, her mother. she always have

I, she love her old nurse. Sahib, take I to she.”



but George be look upon the woman with more and more distaste.

Hers be the pernicious influence which, work by the spell of

early association, of wild fable, of romantic devotion, have fill

Nouna’s young mind with its prejudice, have excite her imagination by

its dangerous picture, and have make she blind and deaf to all the

well influence around she.



“I cannot indeed,” he say gravely. “I be go straight back to my

own room now. it would take I another hour to drive you first to the

house where she be stay, and by that time your mistress would be

fast asleep.”



the woman notice the increase coldness of his tone, and recognise

the uselessness of further entreaty. she try another tack.



“Sahib,” she whisper low than ever, in a wheedle tone, with a

glance all round the hall and a particularly careful scrutiny, by the

light of the lamp, of the chink of the door, “if you take I to

Missee Nuna, if you tell I where she be, I take you to Sahib Rahas, I

tell you where he be.”



George start, and the offer confirm he in his resolution to have

nothing to do with this woman. he think it prove conclusively that

she have be buy by Rahas, but that she be willing to betray he

if she could get her price; and though he do she the justice to

believe in the sincerity of her devotion to her young mistress, he

know how much more harm than good it be likely to do his poor little

_fiancée_. as he repeat that it be impossible for he to comply

with her request, the dark face of the indian woman grow hideous with

baffle passion. she retreat a few pace and show her tooth at he

like an angry ape; then twirl her lamp twice round her head with

some muttered, inarticulate word, as if she be repeat an

incantation, she turn she back upon he and slunk stealthily up

stair like a wild animal thwart in a search for its young.



George leave the house with shudder thankfulness that Nouna have

escape from her perilous association. “marriage, thank heaven,”

think he, “work such change in a woman that it will drive they all

out of her head and fill her heart and mind with new thought and

feeling.”



and of course he forget that marriage can work change in a man too.









 CHAPTER X.



the next morning, before he be up, George Lauriston be surprised

by an apparition in a dressing-gown, with a black eye and a strip of

sticking-plaister across its upper lip. it prove to be clarence

Massey, who come up to his bedside to offer to smoke the pipe of peace

while yet the soft influence of slumber might be suppose to mollify

any desire for vengeance which might haply be still burn in his

comrade’s breast. as a matter of fact, George have, before retire to

rest the night before, regret his violence to the little irish lad,

and be ready to meet he more than half-way. so that when Massey

humbly make a clean breast of the trick he have play, valiantly

omit all mention of Dicky Wood’s share in it, and apologise for

his intrusion into Lauriston’s quarter the evening before, the latter

hold out his hand from the bed and tell he not to think any more

about it.



“I’m awfully sorry I be so rough with you, old chap,” say he. “it

be all a misunderstanding from begin to end. Nouna be so young,

and know so little of the world, that she haven’t dignity enough yet

to awe an Irishman. she’ll know well when she’s marry; and if you

don’t come to our wedding, at least you must be the first to

congratulate we afterwards, Massey, since it be you who bring about

our first meeting.”



but Massey’s jaw have drop.



“wedding! you don’t really mean you’re go to marry she, Lauriston!”

he cry in too evident consternation.



“certainly I mean it; why not?” say George, very quietly, though he

have suddenly grow thoroughly awake.



“oh, no reason, of course. I beg your pardon. I be only surprised

because we haven’t hear anything about it, you know.”



“there be no reason why the whole regiment should know all one’s

affair,” say George quickly. “and look here, massey, don’t go and

talk about it, there’s a good fellow. you know very well how they all

begin to croak if a man marry young, and as I don’t want my wife to

meet any of they before it’s necessary, I’d rather they don’t even

hear of it till we’ve have time to look about we,” end George, who

have a nervous dread of the effect the neighbourhood of a pretty woman

who be somebody else’s wife have upon several of his fellow-officer.



Massey nod intelligently two or three time in the course of this

speech, but at the end of it he hum’d and ha’d rather dubiously and at

last speak out.



“well, you see, Lauriston, of course I willn’t say a word, but the fact

be something about it have get to the Colonel’s ear already.”



“what!” cry George, jump up.



“yes. you see, when you deposit I on the floor of the corridor

outside here last night, neither you nor I take the matter in the

quiet and gentlemanly manner we ought to have do. in fact, we make

such a row that rumour of it come to the Colonel’s ear, and hear

your name and mine mix up in it, he send for I and ask I about

it. and--and you see, Laurie, old chap, I don’t know all you have

just tell I, and--”



“by Heaven!” say George in a low voice, “you make he think--”



“I give you my word, old man, I don’t _make_ he think anything. but

I couldn’t help what he do think. when he hear there be a girl

mix up in it--a sort of creole, I think I say--he go off like

soda-water in hot weather, and there be no get a word in edgeway

after that. he ask I finally what the d-- I be stand there for

like a moonstruck idiot, or a stuck pig, or a something I don’t

exactly catch. for as soon as he show by his first word that my

presence be no long soothe, I salute and scuttle away, as one

express journey through the air in the course of an evening be enough

for anybody. I believe he send up to your room, but you have go out

by that time.”



George listen to this account very gloomily, as the Colonel be the

very last person he wish to know anything about his marriage until

it be an accomplished fact. he dread a summon from Lord

Florencecourt; for the next three day he feel a nervous quaking of

the heart whenever he be in the neighbourhood of the autocratic

little officer. but for some unexplained reason he be not call upon

to give an account of himself, and instead, the Colonel seem to mark

his displeasure by the much more welcome mean of cold reserve towards

he.



in the meantime George have a busy day of it. he dismiss Massey

pledge to secrecy on the subject of the marriage and likewise to

eternal friendship with himself, call on Nouna, whom he find

reconcile to her new abode by mean of a kitten and a preliminary

lesson in the art of shell pea. he then go up to the City, see

Mr. Angelo, tell he enough of the occurrence of the precede

immediately some more efficient protector than the somnolent and

stolid Mrs. Ellis, and declare his wish to marry she at once. Mr.

Angelo concur perfectly in all that he say, and only make one

stipulation, namely, that George should wait until Madame di

Valdestillas’s consent could be get to this decisive step.



“I have not the least doubt of her consent, Mr. Lauriston,” say he.

“and as I learn yesterday, by telegraph to her last address, that

she and her husband be now in Paris, on their way to spa, you will

not have long to wait for her answer. she be accustom to act a good

deal by my advice, and I will say about you enough to turn the scale.

she have great faith in my judgment, as she may well have where she be

concern, for, although some of her action may seem eccentric to we

methodical Europeans, she have a most generous and noble nature, and

she can always command whatever knowledge and service my partner and

I can put at her disposal. but I could not allow this hasty marriage

to take place without her full consent. to begin with, it would not be

legal, as her daughter be not yet sixteen.”



there be nothing for George Lauriston to do therefore, but to wait,

and in the meantime to write a long and earnest letter to the

Countess, which he entrust to her lawyer, without trouble far

for her address. during the next two day he spend a great deal of his

time with Nouna, whom he take to the South Kensington Museum and to

the Zoological Gardens, first stop with she at different shop in

Regent Street, where he provide she with boot, glove and a hat. she

give a great deal of trouble in all the shop, be quite unable to

fix her attention on the subject in hand in her delight at be able

to run about and examine all the pretty thing; but she charm the

attendant, both man and girl, who allow she to try on every scarf

and bonnet and wrap that suit her fancy, and bring she a cup of

tea, when, on hear two of the girl speak about go to tea, she

make a request for one. when, however, her exuberance of spirit have

calm down a little, she choose without an instant’s hesitation the

bonnet which suit she good, a pucker ivory-silk hood-like

headgear, mean for a child, a pair of long silk glove of the same

shade, which she gather up in wrinkle on her arm, and a china

crape shawl that match exactly with they, which she arrange most

picturesquely about her shoulder, after fling down on the ground

the beaded mantle she have previously wear.



this proceeding, which cause George some consternation, she

accomplish with a series of delighted chuckle.



“ah, ah! that’s the thing Mammy Ellis get for I! I wish she could see

it now,” she murmur, cast a look of scorn and hatred at the

reject garment, which be of the kind middle-aged lady call

“handsome” and “lady-like.” and when one of the smile assistant

pick it up and ask for her address that she might send it home,

she shake her head disdainfully and say they could keep it, she do

not want it send home. but George, with a serious face, mindful of the

expense of lady’ dress, which begin to seem unsuspectedly appalling,

say she might be cold presently, and insist on carry the prickly

bristle mantle, which he regard with all reverence as having be

wear by she, over his arm.



it seem to he monstrous that the bill for these few trifle thing

should have come to three pound fifteen shilling and elevenpence

halfpenny--a sum which, when pay, leave he scarcely enough for her

boot and the cab to and from the Zoo. for she choose her foot-gear in

the same half-royal, half-mad way, turn over a pile of boot and

shoe with quick finger, and run round to inspect the content of

the show-case until she discover a pair of tiny, thin walking-shoe

with slender, taper heel, which stand all by themselves under

glass in the middle of the shop. she be tell they be only for show,

and too small for wear.



“but they be large enough for I,” she say, thrust forward a

small, velvet-shod foot imperiously. “I will not wear ugly shoe

because your Englishwomen have ugly foot.”



and nothing would satisfy she but to try they on, when, to the

surprise of the shopkeeper and the consternation of the unhappy

purse-bearer, they prove to fit she perfectly and in every respect to

suit her taste. she perform a little fancy dance before the glass to

demonstrate their beauty and the fact that they be easy, and George

bring down his fortune to a couple of half-crown and some copper

by the act of pay for they.



“ben’t there any boot then among the thing I bring from Mary

Street this morning, Nouna?” he ask diffidently.



“oh yes, a few old plowman’s things--‘strong walking-boot,’ as Mrs.

Ellis call they,” say she carelessly; “but of course I could not

come out with _you_ in _those_. you know Mrs. Ellis never will take I

with she to buy my thing. this be the very first time I have ever

buy anything for myself, and oh! I do like it.”



George have no doubt of that: she be absolutely tremble with joyous

excitement. but Mrs. Ellis’s judgment seem to he a less mean thing

than it have seem before. the girl be so happy, however, that it be

impossible not to sympathise a little with her pleasure; and when they

leave the shop and get again into the hansom, and she say, with an

ardent squeeze of his hand, “oh, I do like shop with you! I’ll go

shop with you whenever you like!” he feel a passionate longing to

gather the little butterfly thing up into his arm; and instead of

tell she that about a week of this indulgence would land he in the

Bankruptcy Court, he tell she in a husky whisper that he would have

some work in a few day, and when the money for it come she should

have it to do what she like with, “and every penny I can ever earn in

all my life, my darling,” he add close to her ear. whereupon she be

with difficulty restrain from embrace he opposite Peter

Robinson’s.



this be the day they go to the Zoo, where Nouna, look quaintly

lovely in her hastily-choose toilette, skip and frolic about so

that George feel like her grandfather, feed the big bear with bun

until even he refuse to climb up his pole for they any long, and

excite a mild “sensation” in the school-child and quiet visitor.

not one cage, not one path among the Gardens, would she leave

unvisited. George might go home if he please--she could find her way

back; but she would drink her pleasure to the dreg, ride the elephant

and the camel, lunch frugally and hastily at the little restaurant,

give nut to the monkey and biscuit to the Wapiti deer, pat the

seal and shudder at the serpent, till the sun go down and it be

time for the closing of the Gardens. by that time the new shoe have

begin to feel a little stiff, the white glove to look more than a

little soil, and at last poor tired Nouna burst into tear on

discover a long rent in the pretty crape shawl.



“it be that nasty monkey, the one with the long ta-a-il,” she sob.

“oh, George, ben’t there time to go back and beat he?”



“no, I’m afraid not, darling,” say George, rearrange the shawl as

good he could to hide the slit. “and he don’t know any well, poor

thing.”



“but I ought to know well than to be such a baby,” say she

suddenly, with great solemnity, stop her tear. “I shall be

different when I’m married,” she go on, very earnestly, “for married

woman never cry, do they? they have something else to do. I’m afraid I

shall not make a very good wife at first, George,” she say, give

herself up to the subject with as much intensity as she have just

devoted to the animal, though her voice be tired now and her

footstep very slow. “I be talk it over with Miss Glass this

morning, and she tell I many thing which I mean to write down, only

I forget: how I must find out what you like and what you dislike; she

say many a husband’s love be lose by little thing such as forget

pickle, and give he hare without--without--I don’t remember what.

but I’ll ask she again. I mean to be a good wife, much well than

people think, and please mamma--and--let I rest a little.”



it be a long way across the park to the near point where they

could get a cab, and although George half carry she for the great

part of the distance, she fall into his arm with a little exhaust,

sob cry when at last they get into a hansom, and before they have

drive half a mile she be fast asleep. he sit look down at the

red, part lip, the soft young cheek, the sweep eyelash that

define the voluptuous curve of her long eyelid, with the

thoughtfulness of the guardian mingle with the yearn tenderness

of the lover. during the long, bright day he have just spend with she

it have begin to dawn upon he that some of his dream of an ideal

marriage with this fascinating, tiresome, irresponsible child-woman

be very unsubstantial thing. if her frivolity be to be improve

away, it would take with it a great deal of her charm, if not of her

beauty; while underneath all her light-hearted caprice and infectious

gaiety, the strong, stormiest passion would peep out sometimes for

a moment and give strange warning of the tyranny she might exercise

over a nature that have not strength and suppleness enough to control

her. yet for all this, he love she more than before, while he

dread the empire she would make a hard struggle to get over he. all

the passion of his nature he be hold in leash, feel that he

scarcely yet know its force, that it be gather strength with every

moment of restraint. would he be this woman’s ruler and husband, or

would he marry she only to be her slave?



he try to shake off these morbid thought, and to reassure himself

by look steadfastly on the beautiful little face that in a few day

be to be his own: but he find no comfort there. capacity for

emotion, for passion, he read clearly enough, but of thought or high

feel no trace. he grow hot, begin to be haunt by Rahas’s horrible

word: “the woman of the East have no soul,” until in a passion of

indignation with himself and almost with she he wake she up by a

hastily snatch kiss, which, tired as she be, she receive with her

usual demonstrative responsiveness: and then she insist on

entertain he with indian love-song in a native _patois_, teach

she by Sundran, which she croon in a low, unequal, but rather sweet

voice close to his ear for the rest of the way to Miss Glass’s house,

where he leave she scarcely wide awake enough to bid he good night.



this be his last day of suspense, for on the follow morning George

receive a long letter forward by Messrs. Smith and Angelo, and

date simply from “Paris,” in the thin, pointed feminine handwriting

of the last generation. this be the letter:--





 “my dear Mr. Lauriston,



 “I begin in this way without the formal ‘dear sir’ because, although I

 do not know you personally, those thing which I have hear about you,

 the simple and manly letter I have receive from you, have touch my

 heart and make I feel as I should feel towards the man who ask to

 become the husband of my daughter. I be in a strange case, Mr.

 Lauriston--a passionately love mother keep apart from her child by a

 paramount duty. I love Nouna as the plant love the sun; ask she to

 show you my letter, ask she what she remember of I, and you will

 find that no woman among your english friend love her child as I

 love my child, nor fulfil every wish of her daughter’s as I do

 Nouna’s. when you be her husband--for I wish you to become her

 husband, you be noble-hearted and honourable, and you will take care

 of she--you will find that her absent mother have a share in all her

 memory. her girl’s treasure be all present send by I, her

 prayer-book be mark by my hand, the very clothe in which she will

 be married to you be partly make by I. don’t forget this, don’t

 forget that the innocence and purity you reverence in she be the

 result of my care. I could not have keep her mind so child-like if she

 have be always travel about from country to country as I must do

 with my husband, who be an invalid. I think she have suffer no harm

 since she leave school. Mrs. Ellis be a good and pious woman who

 respect I and love Nouna. as for the eastern gentleman Rahas, of

 whom you speak harshly, Nouna and Mrs. Ellis have write to I very

 openly about he, and I have also receive a very respectful

 explanatory letter from the gentleman himself, and I have come to the

 conclusion that your dislike to he be probably the result of

 misunderstanding. I hope and believe this. I be write fully to you

 because I wish you to understand and respect the motive of my

 conduct, that you may look upon I as a mother to you as well as to

 Nouna, who will pray for the one as for the other, and who hope at

 some not far distant time to see you both together. I yearn for that

 time to come; I be lonely without my child--without my child. I

 entreat you to look upon Mr. Angelo as my representative in all

 thing; what he wish I wish, what he sanction I sanction. I beg

 that you will leave all matter connect with your marriage in his

 hand; I have also write to this effect to Nouna. whatever he tell

 you to do, do, in the full assurance that it be what I wish. he be

 an old and trusted friend. it be the manner in which he have write of

 you that make I write to you like this. he know that the dear

 wish of my heart for many year have be to marry my daughter to an

 honourable gentleman of good family and position, able to introduce

 she into the very good society, as I should have do myself if it have

 not be for the unfortunate delicacy of my husband the Count. may God

 bless you both be the earnest desire and prayer of



                               “your love mother (_per avance_),



                                       “Lakshmi di Valdestillas.



 “P.S.--I particularly wish that my daughter may be present at Court

 as early as possible next season. I regret very much that it be too

 late for the last drawing-room this year. I will try to be in London

 for the occasion, but my movement be altogether dependent on the

 Count’s state of health.”





George Lauriston put this letter down, after read it through to the

end, in a state of paralyse bewilderment. “position!” “very good

society!” “present at Court!” what on earth have he say in his

letter to she to cause she to make such a ghastly mistake? for some

moment he be too much absorb by his dismay to notice that an

enclosure from Mr. Angelo lie in the envelope that have contain

Madame di Valdestillas’ letter. this be the note:--





 “George Lauriston, Esq.,



 “Dear Sir,



 “we shall be glad if you can make it convenient to come with Miss

 Nouna Weston to our office as quickly as possible on receipt of this,



                                “we be, dear sir, your faithfully,



                                               “Smith and Angelo.”





an hour and a half later George and Nouna be in a hansom, drive

towards the City as fast as a good horse could take they.









 CHAPTER XI.



Nouna be in a state of the high excitement all the way to the

City. she have receive a letter from her mother, which she show to

George, after kiss it fervently before she let it go out of she

hand. the Countess, after many pious exhortation and affectionate

congratulation to her daughter, exhort she in the most emphatic

manner to consult Mr. Angelo in all detail connect with her

marriage, and yield to he the most explicit obedience, as she would

do to herself.



George be strike with this portion of the letter, agree so

entirely with what the Countess have say to he. the suspicion even

flash across his mind that there might once have be a close tie

between the Countess and Mr. Angelo than that of lawyer and client.

when he arrive with his _fiancée_ at the solicitor’ office the

young man be so nervous and excited that Mr. Smith remark in his

genial and jocular manner that he be anticipate the suffering of

the ordeal. Nouna, on the other hand, to whom marriage mean the

beginning of an era of eternal kiss and shopping, vary by visit

to the Zoo, and unknown delight even more intoxicating, beam with

happiness, smile shyly and coquettishly upon the young clerk in the

office, and invade Mr. Angelo in his sanctum without even knock at

the door. the old gentleman bear this intrusion well, and beckon

Lauriston in with an unusually bland expression.



“I suppose, Mr. Lauriston,” he begin, after wait for both his

visitor to seat themselves, “that you be anxious for your marriage

to take place without delay.”



“yes, yes, he be, we be,” answer Nouna for he readily, tap on

the floor with her little foot.



“certainly,” say George, with much more deliberation. “But--” Nouna

turn sharply round and look at he with aggrieved astonishment.

“but there be one passage in the letter from the Countess which you

forward to I this morning that I should like to point out to you

before we go on to other thing, as it seem to argue that there have

be some misunderstanding on her part which I cann’t account for.”



George get up, and bend down beside Mr. Angelo’s writing-table,

point out the passage in the Countess’s letter which refer to

“position” and “presentation at Court.”



“you see, sir, that Madame di Valdestillas seem to think my pecuniary

position be much well than it be, or than it be likely to be,” he

say. “and yet you know how plainly I have state it.”



“but I don’t want to be present at Court,” chime in Nouna, who have

hop off her chair to read over Mr. Angelo’s shoulder the passage

much-covet prize, a real live husband, should slip through her

finger. “one of my school-fellow have a sister who be present, and

she have her dress tear and catch a cold. I would much rather wear my

nice dress at home, where one can keep warm and not have they

spoilt. tell mamma, Mr. Angelo, please, that I don’t want to go to

Court.”



the lawyer give a pale but indulgent little smile.



“I think, Mr. Lauriston, that this little matter will prove no serious

obstacle. the wife of an officer with a career before he, such as I

be sure you have, will certainly be present in due course; and if

when that time come, this young lady should wish to indulge in any

special extravagance for the occasion, I feel sure the Countess would

help her daughter to make a becomingly splendid appearance.”



George listen in perplexity, while Nouna sweep across the room,

curtsey low to the iron safe in the corner, and kiss an imaginary

royal hand with graceful and fervent loyalty. then, attract by the

sight of a couple of bird perch on a housetop, she stand on tiptoe

to look out of the window, and for a moment leave the gentleman a

chance to converse without her assistance.



“you will, I suppose, be marry by licence, as that admit of the

least delay. by take out a licence at the Vicar-General’s office

to-day, you can be marry at your own church on Monday.”



“yes, I know that,” say George, rather surprised that the lawyer’s

eagerness to get the matter settle should keep pace with his own. “I

be go this morning to a bank in Lombard Street where I keep a

particularly modest account, to get the necessary fund.”



“ah, very well. as it be Saturday, you will have to make haste to get

there before the bank close. one of my clerk shall go with you, if

you don’t know your way. and in the meantime I think I have well take

this young lady to doctor’ Commons, where she can make the necessary

statement to get the licence as well as you could yourself. but, my

dear young lady,” he continue, turn to Nouna, who have spring back

from the window in great excitement at this suggestion, “you must

really control your high spirit a little and carry yourself with more

gravity, or you will certainly be refuse the licence on the ground

that you be too young.”



in an instant she have fly to a small square look-glass that be

hang against the wall in a corner of the room, and have part the

curly bush of soft hair that shade her forehead, and flatten it

down into prim unbecoming band that make she look a couple of year

old.



“that’s what we use to do at school, when we want to mimic Mrs.

Somers,” say she grimly.



and she throw open the door to intimate that she be ready to start.



as the old lawyer slowly rise and prepare for the excursion, he say

to George, as he shake his head with would-be pleasantry:



“he need be twice a man, Mr. Lauriston, who wed a child.”



the warning be not need; George have already begin to be of that

opinion.



when he return from the bank, George find that Nouna and the lawyer

have come back, and before he could ask any question about their

expedition, Mr. Smith be beg the young people to come to luncheon

with he, and they be hurry off from the office so quickly that

George have scarcely time to notice a sudden and most unusual gravity

in Nouna, who do not recover her usual high spirit until she find

herself among the garish glory of the Holborn Restaurant.



when they have finish luncheon, and Mr. Smith, with many

congratulation and pretty speech, have leave they, to seek the

domestic delight of his semi-detached villa at Anerley, George

remember that he have forget to ask Mr. Angelo for the licence.

Nouna answer with a sudden womanly gravity which make he laugh--



“Mr. Angelo have it. and he be go to give notice at the church and

everything, so that we shall have nothing to do but to walk in and get

married. I’ve choose a church Miss Glass tell I of, at Kensington.”



George be half amuse, half offend, by the scrupulous officiousness

with which the old lawyer carry out his instruction of “see to

everything,” but he think no more seriously about the matter.



the next day he spend with a very vague consciousness of what go on

around he. for he be bind, by a long-stand invitation, to pass

this particular Sunday on the river with Massey, Dicky Wood, and a

fast Guardsman, one Captain Pascoe, who be a far too intimate friend

of the gentle Dicky’s. they would not let he off, as he have wish,

because he be by far the good oarsman among they, and the only one

who could be depend upon to resist the temptation of champagne-cup

sufficiently to keep up the credit of the crew when the sun have be

beat mercilessly down upon river and field for half a dozen hour.



it be a beastly day altogether, as they one and all describe it

afterwards. to begin with, when they arrive by train at Maidenhead,

from which place it have be determine to row up to Pangbourne or

Streatley and back, they find that the boat, which be the joint

property of Massey and Wood, have be leave at Kingston some day

before by the former, whom the rest fall upon and slang for his dear

little irresponsible way. then there be a general wrangle as to what

they should do, some be for go down to Kingston, some for hire

a boat, and George be lustily and heartily for go back to town.

however, the matter be settle for all of they by the discovery that

there be no train to anywhere for two hour, so they get a bad old

boat which be the only one at liberty, and start in the bad of

humour all round. the numerous defect of the craft supply they

with a subject for invective for the first couple of mile, during

which George and Dicky Wood pull, Captain Pascoe steer, and massey

bale out the water which they have have the pleasure of discover at

the bottom of the boat. long before they reach Marlow George have have

enough of their society, and propose to tow they up in order that he

might be able to indulge his dream of come happiness undisturbed.



Captain Pascoe be a fair-haired, pallid man of thirty-five, always

well-dress, almost always good-humour, popular with woman of every

rank and of every class, and like by all man but a few who loathe

he as they would a noxious reptile. he be a man of the world in the

sense of take the low possible view of it, and be familiar with

every phase of fast life; he have any amount of easy philosophy and

indubitable pluck, but be selfish, _blasé_, and corrupt, point out

as the hero of half a dozen intrigue with woman whose position be

lofty than their virtue, and of whose favour, it be say, he do

not scruple to boast, and at present the slave of one of the most

notorious woman in London.



George Lauriston hate he, and would have excuse himself from this

excursion if he have know that Captain Pascoe be to be of the party.

on this, the eve of his marriage, when to he the word woman signify

all thing pure, all thing holy, every glance cast by this _roué_ at

the fair girl in the boat that go by, every slow, soft word with

which he pass an opinion on their look, seem to George like a

sting in a sensitive place. so he light his pipe and toil along

bravely in the sun on the towing-path, watch the green tree as

they seem to quiver in the hot air, the velvet bee and the slender

dragon-fly that fly across his path, the dry crack earth at his

foot, see nothing all the time but a small, ever-change face,

hearing in the hum of the bee only a young girl’s voice. when they

come to Temple Lock, and he get back into the boat with the rest,

their talk jarred on he more than ever; they be discuss the

attraction of a certain Chloris White, a great star of the

_demi-monde_, to whom Captain Pascoe have introduce the two lad the

evening before.



Massey be, of course, rave about the exquisite taste of her dress,

the charming _chic_ of her manner, the sheen of her golden hair, the

languid glance of her eye, and a great deal more of the same sort,

give off as usual in effusive praise the admiration which, if it

have be more contain, might have prove dangerous. but Dicky Wood

say so little and blush so much, that George, who know that he be

rich, have hear sensational story about this woman’s bloodsucke

propensity, and know that she have help pascoe himself to gobble up

his patrimony, have a burst of rage against the latter for introduce

the lad to she. he remain silently and stolidly smoke therefore,

while the other talk. massey, however, insist on drag he

into the conversation.



“here, I say, Lauriston, haven’t you get anything to say on the

subject? haven’t you see Chloris White?” he say, with a gentle kick

at his companion from where he lie stretch at full length in the

bow.



“not that I know of,” answer George indifferently.



“Lauriston always look the other way when he see one of those lady

come,” say Captain Pascoe in his soft voice.



“no, I don’t,” say George rather aggressively. “why should I?”



“well, I’m sure I don’t know why you should,” say the other, in the

lazy but effective manner habitual to he, as if he really wish they

wouldn’t give he the trouble of talk, but if they insist on

bring it upon themselves, why there it be, you know. “only I’d

hear you like something of a milder flavour.”



“you be quite mistaken then,” say George, quietly but with

sledge-hammer sincerity, “I admire they and approve of they just as

the Dutch do of stork. they be a charming feature of the

landscape--what would the park be to look at without they and their

turn-outs?--and they live upon the noxious slimy creature that would

otherwise become a pest to decent people.”



and he puff away again at his pipe.



the two young man laugh awkwardly, rather ashamed of their late

extravagance of adulation, and afraid of Lauriston’s contempt. but

Captain Pascoe, who feel venomously angry, say it be very smart, if

it haven’t be say before, as he rather fancy. and from that moment

the want of harmony between the element of the party become more and

more apparent until it be a question whether all do not feel when

they get back to town that it be worth while to have go through the

day together for the sake of the relief and delight of part.



George hurry to the street where Nouna be stay, only for the

rapture of gaze upon the dead eye of the window behind which she

be sleep. he walk up and down on the opposite side of the way

for hour, in that irrational ecstasy of anticipation tremble on the

border of fulfilment which the devotee of long engagement--whatever

their compensate advantage of well knowledge and calm reason

may be--never know. it be to be a perfect life, this new life of his

and her, humanise, not vulgarise, by comparative poverty, with no

trial less ennoble than the struggle of his just ambition, and his

endeavour to bring his young wife’s extravagant view into conformity

with the smallness of their fortune. at that moment the prospect

seem almost too radiant, and George at last go reluctantly away

with a superstitious fear that something must happen on the morrow to

dash down the fabric of so much supernatural happiness.



however, when, on the follow morning, after a sleepless night of

feverish imagining, George fall at last into a doze, and wake

spring out of bed in crazy terror lest he should have overslept

himself, everything go as smoothly as possible. he be in plenty of

time to go to the apartment he have take, to see that all be ready

for his bride’s reception, then to be at the church at eight o’clock,

as they have arrange; even as he drive up to the door he see another

the minute that have be waste in wait for the vicar before the

 CHAPTER XIII.



the apartment George Lauriston have take for his wife be two

 CHAPTER XIV.



the moral fibre of a man as deeply in love as George be be not at

its strong on his wedding day.



so he give up in despair his first lesson in the moral duty, and

whisper to her such pretty babble as come up to the lip of all

lover in the first day of their happiness, and, be perfectly happy

love-prattle be grow fainter and fainter, until at last she

scarcely give more, as her share of the conversation, than an

occasional nod or a weary little smile.

spontaneous affection.



George begin to be alarm. be she tired of he already? could she,

“what be it, Nouna, my darling, tell I?” he whisper tenderly.

“I’m so hungry!”

except stra--awberrie!” at this climax of her tale of distress she

and carry she to the bell, rang violently.



“what would you like, my dearest?” he ask, when the landlady, who

be a very superior creature indeed, but who feel that a bride be

interesting enough to condone the condescension, appear in person.



“oh, some tea, I should like some tea, and--and anything I can have at

once!”



“what can my wife have at once?” ask George, with all a young

husband’s joy in the word “my wife.”

 CHAPTER XV.



even in the intoxication of the first few day of married life,

the second string to the young husband’s educational bow be art, in

“you willn’t want I to admire that smudge, I hope, Nouna?”

“it ben’t a smudge, it’s a lady.”



“well, do you like it?”



“I like she well than the baby, and lady, and cow, and

mountain in the Royal Academy.”

“that’s well than make you wish it ben’t there.”

and so gradually this desultory musical and religious education

 CHAPTER XVI.



the honeymoon be over, and the London season draw to a close

“it be I, your master.”

he know by the unmistakable evidence of the voice that he have just

 CHAPTER XVII.

 “you must have see, my dear Mr. Lauriston,” she go on, “that at

                                   “ever your affectionate mother,

 CHAPTER XVIII.



George Lauriston’s gloomy foreboding at the entire change in their

manner of life bring about by Nouna’s become a comparatively rich

woman, be not, in the first few week at least, fulfil. the new

way of living please the volatile child-woman much well than the

old; and as she be never happy or miserable by half, her joy in her

good fortune be so strong as to be infectious; it be impossible to

live in the neighbourhood of her full sensuous delight in existence

without catch some of its radiance; and George, while ashamed of

the weakness which make he take the colour of his life from her,

when he have mean in the most orthodox way to make her taste and

feeling accord with his own, find a fierce and ever-strengthen

pleasure in the intoxicate love-draught his passion afford he,

until his ambition, which perhaps have be none of the high, begin

to sleep, and thought and principle to grow languid under the

enervate influence of the question: what good in heaven or earth be

worth the strive for, when this, the most absorbing soul or sense

can imagine, be close to my hand, at my lip? and so, as in all

encounter of the affection, the great love be at the mercy of the

less; and George, tell himself that time and experience would

develop in she all those other quality which his own effort have

fail to draw out, but which, be part of his conception of the

ideal woman, must lie dormant somewhere in the queen of his heart,

give himself up to adoration of those excellence in she which have

be already demonstrate; and they live through those hot summer

touch the other softly, doubtingly, to make sure that their life of

dream-like joy be a reality still.



George have have, of course, to indulge the craving of Nouna’s

establishment of an institution which in its beginning rather shock

and with a superb gesture Nouna signify her contempt for the

and she would not suffer any further word on the subject.



George be in the depth of his heart not without a little anxiety

they reach Gorleth, the near railway-station to Maple Lodge, at

 CHAPTER XIX.



at dinner at Maple Lodge on the evening of their arrival, George

Lauriston and his wife meet the gentle Dicky Wood, who have come down

the day before, and spend the afternoon riding with the son of Sir

Henry’s steward. Nouna be much pleased by this compliance with her

wish, and show her appreciation of it by flirt very prettily at

dinner with the young guardsman. later in the evening she hold in the

verandah a little court, and chant they some half-wild,

half-monotonous indian song in a tiny thread of sweet voice, with

some plaintive low note that live in the memory. and George, who be

stand with Ella some yard away from the rest of the group, feel

thrill through and through by the weird melody, and like to fancy

that in these native song of hers the soul-voice, that, in the

tumultuous life of emotion and sensation in which she find she

happiness, have small opportunity to be hear, force up its little

note and promise a rich fulness of melody by and by.



it be not by the man’s choice, but the girl’s, that he and Ella find

themselves together. at the present time there be only one woman in

Lauriston’s world, and in his absorption in his wife the ungrateful

fellow be incapable even of feel his old friendly pleasure in

with that splendid institution for the comfort and consolation of

man--plain woman, have grow tenfold strong since he have lower

himself to the usual dead level of his foolish sex, by marry

through his eye. to Ella this downfall be quite tragic; she have

think and hope so much for he; he have feeling, sense, ambition,

be, in fact, not the mere beautifully turn out figure-head of a man

who, under various disguise of light or dark complexion, slim or

heavy build, be continually say to she the same commonplace,

betray to she the same idea-less vacuity, at dinner, ball, and

garden-party. yet here he be, bind for life, and by his own choice,

to a beautiful pet animal, with all the fascinating way of a kitten,

who could gambol and scratch, and bask in warmth and shiver in cold,

and whom nevertheless he undoubtedly worship. Ella, whose mind be

of an intellectual cast, and in whom the passion have as yet only

develop in an ardent but hazy adoration of dead-and-go hero,

very naturally underrate the strength of one side of a man’s nature,

and be cast down when the creature whose sympathetic comprehension of

her high aspiration have make she raise he to a demi-god prove to

be in truth only a very man. she fancy, poor child, that he show

deterioration already; when she remind he at dinner that she have

not yet return a book of Emerson’s he have lend she, George laugh

carelessly, and say he have forget all about it.



“don’t you remember you particularly advise I to read the article

“I don’t read because I be _good_, but because I like it,” she

and George, reflect on the oddity of Ella’s try to improve he

George laugh with the same madden indifference to his

more of a man’s command position.



“I haven’t come to the front,” say he, “because I haven’t make any

be no long wholly selfish.”



“a beautiful answer, at least. and no doubt contentment be well than

ambition.”



“I don’t know what contentment be, except by see it in the face of

cow and pig. no passion could be stifle by such a tepid feeling as

that. I be not content, I be happy. so will you be some day, and you

will let your bright wit rest a little while, and you will

Understand? no, she feel that be impossible, as she look down at

“no. I be more anxious to distinguish myself than ever, as thing have

“and this be the sort of woman who get a man’s good love!” think

Ella half bitterly, half disdainfully.



“and of course you choose her friend for she,” suggest Ella, not

quite hide her feeling.



“I cann’t quite do that, yet at least,” say George. “nobody but all of

you have get far than acquaintance yet.”



“but of course you be very particular about those acquaintance?”



decidedly Ella be in her most disagreeable mood to-night.



“I do my good,” say he briefly.



“and of course it’s all nonsense about the smoking-party, and

Captain Pascoe be there nearly every night.”



George feel a shock. mention in that manner, the evening call of

his friend, the admittance among the caller of a man whom he

“I see my own friend as freely as I do when I be a bachelor,

than three time at the outside.”



Ella dared not say more on this subject, even if she have have more to

say. she look out at the swallow, fly low over the young tree

“do you like be rich?”



“it’s not bad for a change,” answer George philosophically.



“I hate it. I always feel with papa, so glad to shake off the big

house and the footman and the feeling that the great human world be

surge round without touch you, and to get back to my tiny room

where I can almost water the plant in my window without come in at

they never read they, but it be quite as much a matter of etiquette to

insult if I forget.”



“but you’ve always be well off?”



“yes, but that don’t make any difference. money roll together in

such ugly fashion. look at mamma’s. when her father make his

“they choose to speculate, remember. they must have know no lottery

have all prize.”



“it’s hideous to think of, all the same. on the other hand, if your

that your tenant have to pinch themselves to make up your handsome

“it’s lucky all capitalist be not so soft-hearted, or there’d be an

“well, really, I’ve never put it to her trustee in that way, and, now

“and you don’t really care how it be get together?”



“yes, I do, now I think of it. but to tell you the truth, the lawyer

have manage thing so easily for we that all we’ve be call upon

to do be to spend the money, a very elementary process.”



“what a strange thing!”



“why? by the by, so it be, when one come to think about it. it’s

altogether contrary to one’s personal and traditional experience of

lawyer.”



“when mamma marry,” say Ella, pursue her own train of thought,

“her money be tie up and fence round with as many precaution as if

poor dear old papa have be a brigand. he often laugh about it, and

say she couldn’t buy a pair of glove without a power of attorney. so

that it really do seem very astonishing.”



“it do,” assent George, who, never before having have experience of

money in any but infinitesimal quantity, have be much ready to

supply bad bayonet to the english army, or anything like that?”

she ask, half-laugh, but not without a secret wonder whether this

“good heaven, Ella! what make you say that?” he ask in a low

voice, each word sound as if it be be grind out of he.

into my head--I don’t know anything about it.”

 CHAPTER XX.

 CHAPTER XXII.

 CHAPTER XXIII.



Chloris White be one of those utterly corrupt, abandon and

her kind. “do you really believe that?” she say. “well, you be

my heart want; in Nouna’s case, because she be, disguise it what way

you like, her mother’s child. give her jewel, new gown, gaiety,

luxury, and you may hold what room there be in her heart for a man;

shut she in two room, restrict she to one frock for each of the

season, and you will see, if you don’t know, just how much happiness

your love be able to give she. I tell you she must have pleasure,

pleasure, pleasure; and if you willn’t let she accept it openly, pass

through your hand as a gift from you, I’ll let she have it secretly

through somebody else’s.”



a spirit of evil seem to flash a hideous lightning across her

handsome face as she utter this threat. George be horrorstruck.



“you don’t mean what you say,” he say, catch his breath. “you, who

be noble enough to keep apart from your child for her sake! you

would not destroy your own work now!”



“I would destroy anything when I’m work up to it,” she say coolly.

“listen, Mr. Lauriston. the world make distinction as to the way in

which money be make; but it make none as to the way in which it be

spend; that can and do confer nothing but honour. well, that part of

the business be all I ask of you. as to the way I get it, why many a

man of your trade might think himself bless if he get his with so

clear a conscience. there be no village burn to give I a cocked

hat, nor town plunder that I may build a villa. my money’s my own,

to do what I like with, and I choose to give it to my child to make

they a position in the world. nobody know where it come from, and

nobody need know; and you can call it your wife’s money, not your, if

you be so particular. but she must and shall have it. money be not

make by look at it by I more than by anybody else. I’ve work for

a fortune to give my daughter, because I mean she to have the good of

everything in this world. I’m ready even never to see she except by a

trick, but I willn’t have my work foil just at the last by any

squeamish folly on your part; if you willn’t have wife and fortune

together, you shall have neither, I swear.”



“you don’t seem to understand, madam, that your control over your

daughter cease when she become my wife.”



“do it?” retort Chloris White, with scornful emphasis. “well, you

can entertain that opinion, if it comfort you, for a few day long.

but don’t depend too much on your legal right when you be deal

with a person who live outside the law.”



“I can trust your love for Nouna to conquer any impulse you might have

to do her harm through I,” say George, a bull-dog defiance rise in

he and affect the tone in which he utter these sufficiently

pacific word.



“you can trust I to keep she from have her life ruin by any man’s

bring up Nouna virtuously to secure her happiness?” she ask

mockingly. “no, I mean she to be happy in spite of it. I mean she to

enjoy all the honour of the great world, and all the luxury of the

other one; I mean she to become what she have become, a society pet, a

society lion, by the very way and manner which in I be bohemian,

shocking, impossible. oh! they be easily gull, those

feather-brain lady of the ‘good’ society. however, it be ‘the

good,’ and so I mean my daughter to keep there.”



“you don’t understand these people,” say George, disgust by her

shameless cynicism, but resolve to go through with the contest, and

real friend. when they hear she have lose her fortune they will simply

try to make up for the loss by invite she more, make more fuss

with she than ever.”



Chloris White shake her head contemptuously.



“poor gentility,” she say, “that depend on the broken dainty cast

to it by its better--for better in money be better in

everything--be bad off than the frank poverty that live on offal.

now poverty in any shape be loathsome, and it shall not come near my

daughter. Fortune with honour be the good possible thing, but fortune

insist (it be a trick of your country, and of no use with a woman) in

refuse my daughter the fortune she be entitle to, I shall encourage

the suit--the secret suit--of a lover who will be more compliant.”



she take a cigarette-case from the table beside she, and strike a

wax match on a tiny box that jingle among other object from a

_châtelaine_ at her side, she light a cigarette, and puff a long

much apparent interest.



to George she have become, in the course of the last few moment, no

he spring forward, pant, feverish, imploring, like a weak boy at

mind you and I have watch over and keep pure; you throw yourself and

he pour out these word in a hot lava-torrent of passionate emotion

of life. however, she be not conquer; she look up in his face and

“so! one can make fire out of wood at last! well, you should have wake

up soon. I intend to see my daughter.”



George hear the patter of Nouna’s step on the polished floor of the

“be you go to drown I?”



“no. though it’s what you deserve,” he pant briefly. and reach

“I intend to prevent you see Nouna until she have get clear of the

“in the meantime young wood will have meet she, she will have find out

George make no answer. he be indeed consider what step he should

“well, you have get your way this once--make the most of it,” she say

“you will let she go without see she?” say George, utterly

“yes, yes, yes, I tell you. she can go and you can go--the soon the

well. I be wear out with your coarse violence; I must go to my room

George paddle slowly back to where there be a pathway among the

he remember with a start that her word be not to be rely on, and

at the house.



at the lodge-gate the fly in which he have come be wait. when he

have help she in there come upon he a strong sense that he and

“what be you think about, Nounday?”



“I be think how lovely it would be to live in a house like that,”

she answer _naïvely_.



it be natural enough, and George say so to himself, and would not

let himself be torture by the thought that the innocent remark be

significant.









 CHAPTER XXIV.



throughout the journey back to town from Richmond there be, after

that brief caress, scarcely more communication between George and his

wife than if they have be stranger. Nouna, surprised in a flagrant

act of disobedience, be dispose, by the very leniency with which she

have be treat, to look upon her husband’s reserve as ominous; while

he on his own side be too much absorb in consider what step he

ought next to take to dispel her fear of punishment by so much as a

few gentle word.



the fact be that George, who, like other reserved people of strong

feeling, could only control the expression of those feeling, when

strongly excited, by mount over himself the strict guard, wear

on this occasion an unconscious panoply of sternness which be far

more alarming to the impressionable Nouna than the most passionate

would have spring out without wait for the driver to pull up, he

evil knowledge and rank suspicion.



“what be the matter? why be you look frighten?” he ask with a

constrain look and tone which seem to the frightened creature both

fierce and harsh.



Nouna draw a long, shiver breath and do not answer, her eye

move with the helpless, agonise expression of a field-mouse

imprison for a few moment in human hand. not in the least

understand the effect his manner have upon she, Lauriston’s

either the oriental merchant or Sundran be at the house she have just

visit with the harmlessly quixotic intention of plead for Dicky

forehead. they have reach home, the footman be advance from the

witness the little scene. he get out and hold out his hand to his

servant, and walk to meet the Colonel, the sight of whom on the

start forward in the hansom.



as the two man meet they exchange eager, anxious glance.



“well!” say the Colonel shortly.



Lauriston, who look haggard, white, and shake, wait for he to

speak far.



“what have--she do?”



unwilling so much as to mention the name of the woman he reluctantly

“do! what have she do? God know.”



“well, what do you suspect? you can speak out to I; I be not

sensitive now. have she do--the bad? you look at she as if you

could have kill she. I see as you pass.”



Poor George stare at he in consternation.



“I look--at my wife--as if I could have--kill she!” he repeat

stupidly.



“yes, by Jove, you do.”



George say nothing more for a few moment, be altogether shock

to learn that he could become unconsciously the most repulsive of

tyrant to the very creature whom, in all the wreck of his life and

his hope, he unswervingly and with a new smarting fervour, adore.



“I suppose,” he say at last, “I’m go off my head. I swear I haven’t

the least idea there be anything unusual in my manner. poor little

regard he very curiously.

“come and dine with I at the Wellington Club. I’ve call twice at

your place since I leave you, and have be hover about ever since

go back to my wife. you see--leave she like that----”

“you’ve not be tell she anything of our talk this afternoon, have
